The structure of the text are sections that begin with ----, followed by a single line containing the file path and file name, followed by a variable amount of lines containing the file contents. The text representing the Git repository ends when the symbols --END-- are encounted. Any further text beyond --END-- are meant to be interpreted as instructions using the aforementioned Git repository as context.

chapters/introduction.tex
\chapter{Introduction}\label{chapter:introduction}

Over the last few decades, \ac{pas} has established itself as a non-destructive method for defect spectroscopy of bulk materials in the field of solid state physics and materials science \cite{hugimage}. The high sensitivity of positrons to the electron momentum distribution in a solid makes them exceptionally well-suited probing particles to determine either the electronic structure or the presence of lattice defects. \acs{pas} makes use of the high affinity of positrons to open volume defects \cite[pp. 191f]{coleman} like vacancies, dislocations or grain boundaries. Besides positron lifetime spectroscopy, Doppler-Broadening Spectroscopy (\acs{dbs}) of the positron annihilation line has become a powerful technique for defect analysis.


A typical variable monoenergetic positron beam allows for mean positron implantation depths ranging from the surface to several $\si{\micro m}$. When annihilating with the electrons in the material, the implanted positrons usually emit two Doppler-shifted $\SI{511}{keV}$ gamma quanta. Their energy shift depends mainly on the electron momentum distribution present at the annihilation site. \acs{dbs} is applied to analyze the shape of the Doppler-broadened $\SI{511}{keV}$ photopeak using lineshape parameters and to quantify the fraction of annihilating positrons trapped in defects. 

The "Physics with Positrons" group implemented an analysis tool for positron depth profiling data, called \acs{limpid}. Using implantation and diffusion theory of positrons, it enables the extraction of parameters like the positron diffusion length, which is related to the vacancy concentration \cite[p. 98]{krause-rehberg},
and the individual layer thicknesses of multi-layered samples. The characterization of thin film systems by depth dependent \acs{dbs} is, however, not straightforward since the probed volume is defined by the broad positron implantation profile blurred even further by their subsequent diffusive motion \cite{REINER2012104}.

Within this thesis, a set of vapor-deposited metallic thin-film systems is measured using \acs{dbs} in order to perform different fits to the obtained depth profiles with \acs{limpid}. Additionally, the diffusive behaviour of positrons at interfaces of materials with different positron affinities is investigated. Suitable sample materials for these purposes are Cu and Cr.

To complementarily determine the layer thicknesses obtained by \acs{limpid} we perform Rutherford Backscattering Spectrometry (\acs{rbs}). \acs{rbs} enables the depth-dependent detection of heavy chemical elements by measuring the energy of light backscattered projectile ions \cite[p. 46]{wang2010handbook}. \acs{dbs} and \acs{rbs} have been used as complementary analysis methods before \cite{KINOMURA2012151, DHAKA, GUTIERREZ201257}, e.g., for the characterization of defects in ion implanted SiC \cite{ANWAND1999148}.
----
chapters/conclusion.tex
\chapter{Conclusion and Outlook}\label{chapter: conclusion}

In the present work, vapor-deposited, metallic thin-film systems were measured using two different analysis methods. We chose Cu and Cr as a combination of materials suitable for both \acs{dbs} and \acs{rbs}. The major selection criteria were positron affinity and atomic mass.

First, we measured the depth dependent Doppler-broadening of the positron annihilation line. By averaging five $S(E)$ depth profiles in each measurement we were able to observe the stability of the \acs{dbs} measurements. We used \acs{limpid} to fit different physical models to the data. The annihilation fractions calculated from the fit parameters were considered in the interpretation of the $S(E)$ depth profiles. The depth profiles show the transition from annihilation at the surface to annihilation in the vapor-deposited layers until they converge toward the bulk value of the substrate. $S(W)$ plots provide additional information about the positron states, i.e. annihilation events with high energy electrons, and were used to visualize that the data points are a superposition of the surface state and the different bulk annihilation states.

We were able to show that \acs{limpid} correctly models the diffusion toward materials with higher positron affinity $|A_+|$. The effect was, however, suppressed by the short diffusion length in the vapor-deposited films and therefore only clearly observable in the bilayer samples. The values obtained for the positron diffusion lengths seem reasonable. 

Except for the Cu/Si sample, comparative \acs{rbs} measurements yielded different results for the layer thicknesses. With regard to the determination of the layer thickness, \acs{rbs} is the more accurate method than the \acs{dbs} analysis using the applied physical models. The main shortcoming of the \acs{dbs} analysis compared to \acs{rbs} is the diffusive motion of positrons in the specimen which blurs their broad implantation profile even further. However, the results obtained from \acs{limpid} for the layer thicknesses are still in the correct order of magnitude. Contamination of the samples with O and C, various defect species being present in the sample, a gradient of the defect concentration as well as interface effects all have an impact on the positron trapping behavior. Hence, they affect the outcome of the \acs{limpid} analysis in terms of diffusion length and layer thickness. 


The accuracy of the \acs{limpid} fit results could be improved by using a material combination where the S parameters of all layers are directly visible in the depth profile \cite{PhysRevB.35.4606}. Implementing additional effects for positrons in matter like reflection or channeling and implantation profiles based on Monte-Carlo simulations might further improve the data analysis. The valley-to-peak ratio is sensitive the formation of positronium at the surface which is correlated to the fraction of positrons diffusing back to the surface \cite{krause-rehberg, REINER2012104}. Therefore, measurements of the valley-to-peak ratio could be used additionally for a more precise determination of the positron diffusion length of the top layer.  %p.83f and p.99

chapters/sample_preparation.tex
\chapter{Sample Preparation}\label{chapter: samplepreparation}

\section{Selection of Suitable Materials}

In this work, we analyze positron depth profiles of thin, metallic bilayers on a substrate using \acs{dbs} and \acs{rbs}. Therefore, we need a combination of two materials suitable for both measurement methods. Several characteristics must be taken into account:

\begin{itemize}
\setlength\itemsep{0em}
    \item \textbf{Surface oxide layer} \\
    In general the material must not form a thick surface oxide layer in order to avoid the consideration of an extra surface layer in the \acs{dbs} analysis.
    \item \textbf{Interdiffusion} \\
    The sharpness of the material interface is diminished by interdiffusion during or after sample production. Interdiffusion describes the process of diffusional exchange of atoms across the interface of two materials. This effect is difficult to consider in the \acs{dbs} analysis since we model the data using a sample object consisting of the two different layer materials.
    \item \textbf{S parameter} \\
    For the analysis of the \acs{dbs} measurements to be successful, the two materials must have different S parameters. The S parameter is correlated, among other factors, to the atomic number $Z$ of an element. 
    \item \textbf{Positron affinity} \\
    The difference in positron affinities $A_+$ of the two materials should be sufficiently large, so that we can investigate their influence on the positron motion at the interface.
    \item \textbf{Positron penetration depth} \\
    The penetration depth of the positron beam must be at least as large as the total layer thickness of the sample. A good measure for this is the mean implantation depth as defined in equation \ref{eq: z_mean}. Different mean implantation depths for different materials are mainly caused by the varying material densities $\rho$.
    %\item \textbf{Vapor pressure} \\
    %The vapor pressure of a material is a limiting factor when it comes to the vacuum system of the positron beam.
    %A substance with a too high vapor pressure at a given temperature could potentially harm the vacuum or damage the the vacuum.
    \item \textbf{Mass difference} \\
    For the \acs{rbs} measurements, the mass difference of the two elements is crucial. If the masses are too similar, the spectra of the materials constituting the bilayers are not separable because the energy reduction of the scattered projectile depends, among other things, on the mass of the target nuclei. This is described in more detail in chapter \ref{ch: rbs}.
\end{itemize}


Considering all these criteria, chromium and copper seem like a suitable choice. Their atomic numbers are $24$ and $29$ respectively. Experience has shown that their S parameters differ sufficiently, with Cu exhibiting the lower S parameter. Their positron affinities are $\SI{-2.62}{eV}$ and $\SI{-4.81}{eV}$ \cite{puska_aff}, respectively, so they differ by $\SI{2.19}{eV}$. Regarding the positron penetration depth, we get a mean implantation depth of, for instance,  $\bar{z} = \SI{147}{nm}$ at $\SI{10}{keV}$ and $\bar{z} = \SI{919}{nm}$ at $\SI{30}{keV}$ in Cu according to equation \ref{eq: z_mean}  and the values from table \ref{tab: Makhov parameters}. Similar values are obtained for Cr since its density does not differ much from Cu (see table \ref{tab: sampleprepparam}). Since typical implantation energies for \acs{dbs} reach up to $\SI{35}{keV}$, the positrons penetrate up to about $\SI{1}{\micro m}$. Reaching the bulk of a suited thin film, i.e. neglecting positron back diffusion to the surface, is therefore not an issue.
%Moreover, we compare the vapor pressures of Cu and Cr to Al which is a material that is unproblematic with regard to the vacuum system of the experimental setup of the \acs{lab-beam}. Higher temperatures are required for Cu and Cr to reach the same vapor pressure as Al. Al reaches $\SI{10}{mm Hg}$ at $\SI{1780}{^\circ C}$, whereas Cu and Cr reach this value at $\SI{1870}{^\circ C}$ and $\SI{1840}{^\circ C}$, respectively \cite{haynes2016crc}.



The atomic masses of Cr and Cu are $\SI{52}{u}$ and $\SI{63.5}{u}$, respectively. Simulation of \acs{rbs} spectra using SIMNRA \cite{mayer} ensured that the peaks created by Cu and Cr are still separable. This is exemplary demonstrated for a $\SI{100}{nm}$ Cu layer on a $\SI{100}{nm}$ Cr layer on Si in figure \ref{fig: RBSsimulation}.
The relevant quantities for Cr and Cu used for the selection of materials are summarized in table \ref{tab: sampleprepparam}.

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{|l|c|c|}
\hline
\textbf{Parameter}      & \textbf{Cr} & \textbf{Cu} \\ \hline
Atomic number      &  24  &  29  \\ \hline
Atomic mass (\si{\atomicmassunit})   &  52     &  63.5  \\ \hline
Density ($\si{g/cm^3}$)  & 7.15 & 8.96 \\ \hline
$A_+$ (\si{\electronvolt})   &  -2.62 & -4.81 \\ \hline
$\bar{z}$ (\si{\micro\metre}) at \SI{35}{\kilo\electronvolt} &    1.47 & 1.19   \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption[Material parameters of Cr and Cu taken into account for the selection of sample materials.]{Atomic number, atomic mass, density, positron affinity \cite{puska_aff} and mean implantation depth for Cr and Cu.}
\label{tab: sampleprepparam}
\end{table}


\section{Electron Beam Physical Vapor Deposition (\acs{pvd})}

Evaporation of a material and its subsequent condensation on a substrate is a simple method for thin film deposition. Intense beams of high-energy electrons are commonly utilized to vaporize materials. When the electron beam reaches the surface of the evaporating material, the kinetic energy of the electrons is partly converted into thermal energy which is used to melt or sublimate the material to produce the desired vapor pressure \cite[p. 400]{harsha}.
The vapor then expands into an evacuated chamber that contains the substrate. Since the substrate is at a lower temperature than the evaporation source, the vapor condenses on the substrate.

It is desirable to have a mean free path of the evaporated atoms larger than the distance to the sample in order to minimize scattering with the residual gas. This improves the deposition efficiency, prevents unwanted reactions in the vapor phase and allows precise pattering of films using shadow masks, if desired.
%The mean free path scales inversely with pressure.
Moreover, the contamination of the surface to be coated should be kept at a minimum. In case of poor vacuum in the chamber, chemical reactions taking place at the surface, like oxidation, are more likely.

It is usually assumed that the material leaves the source in the electron beam system as individual atoms. However, at high rates of deposition, the density of vapor atoms might be high enough to create clusters of atoms which could lead to formation of defects or pores in the films \cite[p. 412]{harsha}.
Also, the pressure of the residual gas generally rises if the heating power is increased in order to reach higher deposition rates. This is due to increased desorption of, e.g., water molecules from surrounding surfaces. A lower deposition rate yields better uniformity but an increased risk for contamination. Overall, the optimum deposition rate generally is a compromise between production time, film microstructure, and purity of the film.


\subsection{\acs{pvd} Device at \acs{ipp}}

All metallic thin-film systems studied within this work have been produced at the Max Planck Institute for Plasma Physics (\acs{ipp}) by electron-beam \acs{pvd}. The electron-beam originates from a filament which is not in the line-of-sight of the evaporation point. It has an accelerating voltage of $\SI{7}{keV}$ and is located underneath the evaporation point. A static magnetic field then bends the path of the emitted electrons.

The material to be evaporated is contained in a water-cooled graphite crucible to prevent contamination. Once the temperature is sufficiently high, vapor will result from the melt. The ultrasonically cleaned substrate is placed in the direct line-of-sight of the source at a distance of about $\SI{40}{cm}$. A certain distance between source and sample allows a larger deposition area and limits sample heating by radiation from the source.

During deposition, a quartz crystal was used to measure the current deposition rate. The layer thickness could then be determined using the density of the materials, a factor that accounts for the mechanical %and elastic
properties of the material, and a tooling factor which describes the geometry of the evaporation chamber.

Multiple substrates can be mounted simultaneously on the substrate holder with small screws. Two electron guns can be used simultaneously or, as in the present case, sequentially without breaking the vacuum of the \acs{pvd} system.


\subsection{\acs{pvd} of Cu and Cr on Si Substrates}

Bilayers in two different arrangements on a silicon substrate were produced using the \acs{pvd} device at \acs{ipp}: Cu/Cr and Cr/Cu with a layer thickness of approximately $\SI{100}{nm}$ each. For reference purposes we also produced single layers of Cu and Cr on the same kind of Si substrate with a thickness of approximately $\SI{500}{nm}$. The four sample types are shown schematically in figure \ref{fig: samples}. We produced three samples of each of the four types. All samples were vapor deposited on B-doped Si substrates of $10 \times 10 \times \SI{0.4}{mm}$ size and $\SI{10}{\Omega cm}$ specific resistance. The substrates were cut out of a Si wafer.

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{Images/samples.pdf}
    \caption[Schematics of vapor-deposited monolayer and bilayer samples.]{Diagram of vapor deposited thin-films on a substrate (B-doped Si). The desired thickness of the Cu and Cr monolayers is $\SI{500}{nm}$. For the bilayers, we aim for a layer thickness of $\SI{100}{nm}$ each.}   
    \label{fig: samples}
\end{figure}

Before each deposition, the chamber walls were plasma cleaned for $\SI{10}{min}$. The deposition rates and the characteristics of the vacuum for all vapor deposited films are listed in table \ref{tab:deposition properties}. The strongly varying deposition rate during the deposititon of the Cr monolayer ($1$ - $\SI{6}{\angstrom/ \second}$) is due to sublimation of Cr. Accordingly, the evaporation material consists of multiple pieces with poor thermal contact to each other. This leads to inhomogeneous and varying heating of the evaporation material. It should also be noted that we deliberately chose a better vacuum level for the deposition of the Cr layers. For the deposition of the monolayers, e.g., the residual gas pressure in case of Cr was lower by a factor of ten. This was done to compensate the higher reactivity of Cr with oxygen compared to Cu and thereby minimizing the oxygen contamination during deposition of the layer.

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{c|c|c|c|c}
           & Deposition rate $\bigl(\si{\angstrom/ \second}\bigr)$ & $p_1$ ($10^{-7}\,\si{mbar}$) & $p_2$ ($10^{-7}\,\si{mbar}$) & z (\si{nm}) \\ \hline
Cu         &         $3.5$        &       $1.5$          &           $<7$    & 500             \\
Cr         &           $1$-$6$      &        $0.973$         &         $<0.5$     & 500              \\
Cr (Cu/Cr) &        $1$-$2$         &        $<1$         &           $<0.5$    & 100             \\
Cu (Cu/Cr) &         $1$        &         $0.38$        &              $<2.5$    & 100           \\
Cu (Cr/Cu) &          $1$       &         $0.775$        &           $\approx 2$    & 100         \\
Cr (Cr/Cu) &           $1$-$2$      &       $<0.5$          &              $0.3$-$0.4$    & 100          
\end{tabular}
\caption[Deposition rates, residual gas pressures and nominal thicknesses for the deposition of Cu and Cr films on Si.]{Vapor deposition parameters for Cu and Cr on Si: Deposition rate, residual gas pressure before ($p_1$) and during deposition ($p_2$) as well as the nominal thickness $z$ of each deposited layer. The actual layer thickness may vary by up to 20\%.}
\label{tab:deposition properties}
\end{table}

The thickness of the deposited layers was controlled by a quartz thickness monitor. When the desired film thickness of $\SI{500}{nm}$ in case of the monolayers and $\SI{100}{nm}$ in case of the bilayers is reached, we close a shutter to stop the evaporation. The actual layer thicknesses may differ due to miscalibration of the quartz crystal. The deviation from the nominal thickness is estimated to be up to 20\%.

chapters/methods.tex
\chapter{Physical Basics}\label{chapter: theory}

\section{Defect Analysis with Positrons}

Positrons were postulated in 1928 by P. Dirac in connection with his relativistic electron theory \cite{dirac}. In 1932, they were discovered by C. D. Anderson in a cloud chamber \cite{anderson}, for which he was awarded the 1936 Nobel Prize in Physics \cite{nobelprize}.
Being the first ever evidence of antimatter, the positron has the same rest mass and spin as the electron but carries opposite charge. Positrons have limited
lifetime in matter, since they annihilate in the presence of electrons \cite{krause-rehberg}. They are only stable in vacuum ($\tau > \SI{2e21}{yr}$) \cite{schultz}. Intense development of positron beams initially started after the discovery of the reemission of slow positrons with narrow energy distribution from metallic surfaces in the late 1950s and 1960s \cite[p. 2]{coleman}. Since then beam intensities of up to $10^9$ monoenergetic positrons per second at $\SI{1}{keV}$ have been achieved \cite[2008]{Hugenschmidt}. Today positrons are well established as probe particles in the field of materials science where they are used for studying electronic structures, surfaces or lattice defects \cite{coleman}. %e.g. p. 192

\subsection{Radioisotope Based Positron Sources}

Among other processes, positrons can be produced by radioactive sources during $\upbeta^+$ decay.
It takes place in proton-rich nuclides such as \isotope[22]{Na} or \isotope[64]{Cu}. The former is highly suited for positron annihilation studies and has established itself as the isotope most commonly used in research laboratories. This is due to its high branching ratio for positrons of $90\%$ and its long half-life of $\SI{2.6}{a}$. The dominant $\upbeta^+$ decay of \isotope[22]{Na} can be described by the following reaction:

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: Na22 beta decay}
    \ce{^22Na -> ^22Ne^* + \upbeta^+ +\upnu_e -> ^22Ne + \gamma}.
\end{equation}

Because the energy released in the  $\upbeta^+$ decay is distributed among three particles, the emitted positrons exhibit a continuous energy spectrum with an endpoint energy of $\SI{545}{keV}$ in case of \isotope[22]{Na} \cite[pp. 213f]{schatz}. %p.213f
After the emission of the positron, the daughter nucleus \isotope[22]{Ne} exists in a short-lived (\SI{3.7}{ps}), excited state which is then de-excited by emitting a $\SI{1.28}{MeV}$ gamma photon. The gamma photon can function as a start signal in positron lifetime experiments.  


\subsection{Positrons in Matter}

After being implanted into a solid, positrons dissipate their kinetic energy within a very short timescale compared to its lifetime of typically a few picoseconds.
For the remaining lifetime in the order of $\SI{100}{ps}$ they diffuse through the crystal lattice until they finally annihilate with electrons \cite{puska}. At very low incident energies of up to a few $\si{keV}$ some of the positrons annihilate or leave the solid prior to their complete thermalization as so-called epithermal positrons \cite{britton}.


\begin{figure}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{Images/positrons_in_matter.pdf}
    \caption[Diagram of possible processes for positrons in matter.]{Positron interactions with matter: Positrons hitting the surface either are reflected or enter the solid. Non-thermalized positrons can be re-emitted as epithermal positrons or as positronium. Emission as slow positrons, positronium formation and emission of Auger electrons are possible processes if the positron diffuses back to the surface after thermalization. Positrons reaching the bulk either annihilate in their delocalized state or after being trapped in defects. %\cite{hugimage}
    }
    \label{fig: positrons in matter}
\end{figure}

Figure \ref{fig: positrons in matter} sums up the possible processes for positrons in matter. Depending on the implantation energy and several material parameters of the target like density, atomic number or electronic structure, each process occurs with a different probability.

\subsubsection{Implantation and Thermalization}

First, it should be noted that not all positrons enter the sample. The backreflection probability of the positron depends on the atomic number of the material and increases with the incident energy up to a maximum value of about 40\%. After being implanted, the positrons quickly reach thermal equilibrium with the crystal lattice through various scattering processes. During the first $10^{-15}\,\si{\second}$ ionization of atoms, excitation of electrons and production of excitons lead to the loss of most of their energy. In the following $10^{-12}\,\si{\second}$, the positrons excite phonons and plasmons which reduces their energy to thermal energies \cite[p. 98]{coleman}, corresponding to about $\SI{40}{meV}$ ($\approx \frac{3}{2}k_\mathrm{B}T$) at room temperature.
The exact time of the thermalization process depends on the implantation energy and material properties like density and elemental composition.

However, not all positrons reach thermal energies after being implanted. Especially for lower implantation energies up to a few $\si{keV}$ positrons have an increased probability of scattering back to the surface before having thermalized completely.
In case of a material with a positive positron work function, these positrons must have high enough energy so that they can be released from the surface. The total number of surface emitted positrons is made up by backscattered and epithermal positrons as well as thermalized positrons diffusing back to the surface in case the material has a negative positron work function. The overall fraction of surface emitted positrons decreases with increasing implantation energies \cite{schultz}.

\subsubsection{Implantation Profile}
The depth distribution of implanted monoenergetic positrons can be approximated by a Makhovian profile \cite{makhov}:

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: Makhov profile}
    P(z) = \frac{mz^{m-1}}{z_0^m} \exp \Biggl[ -\Bigl( \frac{z}{z_0} \Bigr)^m \Biggr]
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: z_0}
    \text{with} \qquad z_0 = \frac{\bar{z}}{\Gamma \Bigl(\frac{1}{m}+1 \Bigr)},
\end{equation}

where $\bar{z}$ is the mean implantation depth 

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: z_mean}
    \bar{z} = \frac{A}{\rho} E^n.
\end{equation}

In the above equations E is the implantation energy and $\rho$ is the density of the sample. $\Gamma (x)$ is the gamma function

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: Gamma function}
       \Gamma (x) =  \int_{0}^{\infty} t^{x-1}e^{-t} dt.
\end{equation}


A, m and n are the material dependent parameters, which can be determined
using Monte Carlo simulations. Table \ref{tab: Makhov parameters} contains literature values for selected elements.


\begin{table}[h]
    \centering
    \begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|}
    \hline
    Material & $\rho$ $(\si{g/cm^3})$ & $A$ $(\si{\micro g cm^{-2} keV^{-n}})$ & n      & m         \\ \hline
    Si       & 2.33            & 2.48                        & 1.729(12)  & 1.99(11)  \\ \hline
    Cu       & 8.96            & 2.84                        & 1.667(17)  & 1.729(51) \\ \hline
    W       & 19.25            & 5.06                        & 1.498(29) & 1.621(30)    \\ \hline
    \end{tabular}
    \caption[Densities and Makhovian parameters for Si, Cu and W.]{List of densities and Makhovian parameters from Monte-Carlo simulations using GEANT4 for elements Si, Cu and W \cite{dryzek}.}
    \label{tab: Makhov parameters}
    \end{table}

Figure \ref{fig: implantationprofiles} shows exemplarily the implantation profiles for four different energies in copper.

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics{Images/Implantation_profiles.pdf}
    \caption[Makhov implantation profiles in Cu for different kinetic energies.]{Makhov implantation profiles P(z,E) in Cu for four different energies using the parameters from table \ref{tab: Makhov parameters}. The dotted lines each indicate the mean implantation depth.}
    \label{fig: implantationprofiles}
\end{figure}


\subsubsection{Diffusion and Trapping}

After reaching thermal equilibrium and before annihilating with an electron, positrons in matter continue to scatter quasi-elastically off phonons. Scattering off impurities and velocity randomizing scattering with optical phonons and electrons might also play a role for the diffusion of positrons \cite[pp. 106f]{coleman}.
Being charged particles, the positrons are repelled by the nuclei due to the Coulomb force, leading to an increased probability density in interstitial regions between the atoms \cite[p. 194]{coleman}.
The relation between positron bulk lifetime $\tau_\mathrm{b}$ or bulk annihilation rate $\lambda_\mathrm{b} = \frac{1}{\tau_\mathrm{b}}$  and the diffusion length can be expressed via the diffusion coefficient $D_+$ as follows

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: diffusion length}
    L_+ = \sqrt{D_+ \tau_\mathrm{b}} = \sqrt{\frac{D_+}{\lambda_\mathrm{b}}}.
\end{equation}

Positrons may get trapped in open volume defects such as vacancies, dislocations, grain boundaries or voids while diffusing through the crystal lattice. Regions of low atomic density act as attractive potential wells leading to a quantum-mechanical confining \cite[p. 98]{coleman}.
The introduction of defects leads to an increased mean lifetime but to a decreased bulk lifetime $\tau_\mathrm{b}$. Together with the trapping rate $\kappa_\mathrm{d}$, we get the effective diffusion length

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: effective diffusion length}
    L_{\mathrm{eff}} = \sqrt{\frac{D_+}{\lambda_\mathrm{b} + \kappa_\mathrm{d}}}.
\end{equation}

The defect trapping rate is defined as

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: defect trapping rate}
    \kappa_\mathrm{d} = \mu c_\mathrm{v}
\end{equation}

with $c_V$ being the vacancy concentration and $\mu$ the specific trapping coefficient of a single vacancy. 

The average bulk lifetime $\tau_b \approx \SI{100}{ps}$ of a positron in a sample with a low defect concentration results in typical diffusion lengths of $L_+ \approx \SI{100}{nm}$ \cite[p. 10]{krause-rehberg}, but can decrease to a few $\si{nm}$ in the case of saturation trapping. This is the case when the vacancy concentration has exceeded a critical value and thus all implanted positrons get trapped prior to annihilation. Apart from defects, precipitates with higher positron affinity may also act as positron traps.

Thermalized positrons have a certain probability of returning to the surface by diffusion. Different processes are responsible for this: Firstly, reemission into the vacuum as free positrons is possible if the positron work function is negative. Secondly, positrons may get trapped in surface states at the solid-vacuum interface. Finally, the positron may escape from the surface by capturing a near-surface electron and emerge as energetic or thermal positronium \cite{schultz} (see figure \ref{fig: positrons in matter}). %[p.746]

\subsubsection{Annihilation}

An electron-positron pair usually annihilates into two $\SI{511}{keV}$ photons with a $180 ^\circ$ angle due to energy and momentum conservation. The decay of an electron-positron pair with parallel spins into three gamma quanta is also possible but a factor of 372 less likely \cite[pp. 211f]{schatz}, which justifies the restriction on the emission of two gamma quanta. The momentum of both electron and positron is transferred to the emitted $\gamma$-quanta. However, the momentum of the thermalized positron is negligible compared to the electron binding energy. This nonzero momentum leads to a deviation of the emitted gamma quanta from collinearity in the laboratory system. Additionally, it shifts the energy of the annihilation photons by an absolute value of

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: Doppler shift}
    | \Delta E | = \frac{cp_L}{2}
\end{equation}

with $p_L$ being the longitudinal momentum of the annihilating pair with respect to the detector symmetry axis and $c$ being the speed of light.
The change in momentum and the angular deviation of the photons
in the laboratory frame are schematically shown in figure \ref{fig: Doppler_shift} and expressed by $p_L$ and $p_T$, respectively. In the scope of this work, we focus on the Doppler shift $\Delta E$ as a measure of the local electron momentum distribution.

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{Images/dopper_shift.pdf}
    \caption[Electron-positron annihilation in the laboratory frame of reference.]{The non-zero momentum $p$ of the electron-positron pair in the laboratory frame of reference causes a Doppler shift of the annihilation photons proportional to the longitudinal momentum $p_\mathrm{L}$. The angular deviation from the $180 ^\circ$ angle is expressed by the transverse momentum $p_\mathrm{T}$.}
    \label{fig: Doppler_shift}
\end{figure}

The energy shift depends on the momentum of the electron and therefore on the specific positron annihilation site. Compared to the delocalized positron state in the unperturbed lattice, the positron wave function overlaps less with core electrons when trapped in a vacancy. Since core electrons have higher energies than outer-shell electrons, the annihilation radiation is less Doppler shifted for annihilations in, e.g., vacancies.

\subsection{Doppler-Broadening Spectroscopy (\acs{dbs})} \label{DBS}

Doppler-Broadening Spectroscopy (\acs{dbs}) is based on the sensitivity of the positron to the local electronic environment which causes the broadening of the $\SI{511}{keV}$ annihilation photopeak \cite[p. 705]{schultz}.

\subsubsection{S and W Parameters} \label{ch: Sparam}

Apart from the limited detector resolution, the recording of many annihilation events results in a Doppler-broadened peak around at the expected $\SI{511}{keV}$ value due to the non-zero electron energies. A sample with a high defect concentration corresponds to a less Doppler broadened and, thus narrower peak. Annihilation with core electrons in the defect free bulk, however, leads to larger Doppler shifts. Figure \ref{fig: dopplerbroadenedpeak} shows how defect concentrations can affect the shape of the $\SI{511}{keV}$ peak.

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics{Images/doppler_peak.pdf}
    \caption[Doppler-broadening spectrum of the \SI{511}{keV} line.]{Schematic drawing of two Doppler-broadened $\SI{511}{keV}$ peaks. The annihilation photo peak of the sample with high defect density is narrower due to fewer annihilation events with high-energy core electrons. The lineshape parameters S and W are determined by the quotient of the areas $A_S$ and $A_W$ respectively and the area below the whole curve (see equation \ref{eq: SWparameter}).}
    \label{fig: dopplerbroadenedpeak}
\end{figure}

The broadening of the peak is commonly quantified by the lineshape parameters S and W. Both are defined as the ratio of the respective areas $A_S$ and $A_W$ to the area below the whole peak $A_{\mathrm{peak}}$.

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: SWparameter}
    S = \frac{A_S}{A_{\mathrm{peak}}} \qquad \text{and} \qquad W = \frac{A_W}{A_{\mathrm{peak}}}
\end{equation}

Due to $A_S$ being the area around the center region of the peak, higher defect concentrations lead to a higher value for S. Since W covers the fraction affected by larger energy shifts, indicating the annihilation with core electrons, W decreases with an increasing defect density.
It has to be noted that the boundaries of $A_S$ and $A_W$ can in principle be chosen arbitrarily but have to remain constant within the data evaluation. The comparison of S and W parameters from various measurement campaigns is therefore only possible to a very limited extent. Reasonable values specifying the two areas are $E_S = \SI[separate-uncertainty = true]{511\pm1.1}{keV}$, $E_{W, \mathrm{left}}=\SI[separate-uncertainty = true]{507.5\pm0.5}{keV}$ and $E_{W, \mathrm{right}}=\SI[separate-uncertainty = true]{514.5\pm0.5}{keV}$.


\subsubsection{Depth Profiling}

The Makhov implantation profiles for different energies (see figure \ref{fig: implantationprofiles}) show that a variable monoenergetic positron beam enables depth dependent defect spectroscopy. Implantation energies of up to $\SI{35}{keV}$ lead to mean implantation depths of up to several $\si{\micro m}$ depending mainly on the density of the material.
At low energies, most of the positrons are implanted close to the surface. Only a small fraction will annihilate in the bulk area since many positrons will diffuse back to the surface prior to their annihilation. This is due to the attractive surface potential.
The measured $S(E)$ value of a material is therefore a superposition of the unique
surface value $S_{\mathrm{surf}}$ and the bulk value $S_{\mathrm{bulk}}$. $S_{\mathrm{surf}}$ itself comprises annihilation events from all possible surface states. With increasing implantation energies more positrons are implanted further from the surface, reducing their chance to reach it.

The formation of the $S(E)$ value can be understood as an averaging process. In case of a structure comprising of $N$ layers, the S parameter measured for an incident energy $E$ is given by 

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: S_layered}
    S(E)=\sum\limits_{i=1}^N S_if_i(E) + S_\mathrm{surf}f_\mathrm{surf}(E).
\end{equation}

with $f_i$ being the fraction of positrons annihilating in layer i with a characteristic $S_i$ and $f_\mathrm{surf}$ being the fraction of positrons being able to diffuse back and annihilate at the surface with $S_\mathrm{surf}$ \cite{REINER2012104}.

For a homogeneous material, a depth profile shows the transition from a value close to $S_{\mathrm{surf}}$ for low implantation energies to the bulk value $S_{\mathrm{bulk}}$ for high implantation energies. The exact shape of the depth profile is also affected by the diffusion length of the positrons in a certain material. A long diffusion length, for instance, allows positrons implanted at higher depths to reach the surface and hence leads to a smooth $S(E)$ profile.

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.75\textwidth]{Images/S-W-plot.pdf}
    \caption[$S(E)$ and $S(W)$ plots of B-implanted Si and defect-fre Si.]{\acs{dbs} measurements of B-implanted Si (black symbols) and defect-free Si (open symbols) \cite[p. 94]{krause-rehberg}. (a) S parameter plotted as a function of implantation energy. (b) S versus W plot. The defect free sample connects surface and bulk state on a straight line. For the B-implanted Si sample the data points run with increasing positron energy clockwise from the surface, through the defect. S and W are normalized to their bulk values.}
    \label{fig: S-W-plot}
\end{figure}

Figure \ref{fig: S-W-plot}a exemplarily shows the $S(E)$ depth profiles of B-implanted and defect-free Si. Figure \ref{fig: S-W-plot}b visualizes the corresponding $S(W)$ plots \cite[p. 94]{krause-rehberg}. S and W are normalized to their bulk values. The $S(W)$ data points of the defect-free sample connect the surface and bulk states. The B-implantation introduces a new type of defect which leads to deviation from the straight line. With increasing implantation energy, it runs clockwise through three different annihilation states. In general, S versus W plots can be used for defect identification. For more details on how to read and interpret $S(W)$ plots we refer to the book by Krause-Rehberg \cite{krause-rehberg}.


\subsubsection{Positron Affinity}
The positron affinity is a useful parameter describing the behaviour of positrons in heterostructures made of different materials like multilayer systems or precipitates in alloys \cite{puska_aff}.
The positron affinity $A_+$ can be calculated from the electron and positron work functions: $A_+ = - (\Phi ^- + \Phi ^+)$. The positron work function can either be positive or negative and is given by

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: positron work function}
    \Phi ^+ = -D - \mu ^+,
\end{equation}

where D is the surface dipole barrier, which is caused by the spilling of electrons out from the surface, and $\mu ^+$ is the chemical potential of the positron referenced to the mean electrostatic potential in the bulk \cite{coleman}. %p.133
The electron work function is defined analogously. The positron affinity $A_+$ may then be rewritten as the sum of the electron and positron chemical potential

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: affinity}
    A_+ = \mu ^+ + \mu ^- \equiv - (\Phi^+ + \Phi^-).
\end{equation}


Values for $A_+$ for three different elements are listed in table \ref{tab: affinities}. By definition, a negative sign specifies an attractive potential and hence a high positron affinity.

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
Material & $\Phi ^+$ $(\si{eV})$ & \textbf{$\Phi ^- (\si{eV})$} & \textbf{$A_+ (\si{eV})$} \\ \hline
Si       &  $2.04$   &     $4.91(5)$   & $-6.95$        \\ \hline
Cu     &   $-0.3(2)$     &    $4.59$    & $-4.81$        \\ \hline
Cr       &   $-1.76(5)$   &  $4.46(6)$ & $-2.62$        \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption[Literature values of positron affinities of Si, Cu and Cr.]{Literature values of positron affinities of Si, Cu and Cr \cite{puska_aff}. The electron and positron work functions depend on the crystal orientation. Their experimental values are exemplarily given for the $\langle100\rangle$ crystal orientation \cite[pp. 135f]{coleman}.}
\label{tab: affinities}
\end{table}


\subsection{Layer-wise Investigation of Measurements on Positron Implantation and Diffusion (\acs{limpid})} \label{ch: limpid}


\acs{limpid} is a software tool built for simulating positron annihilation depth profiles of single and multi-layered structures and, more importantly, fitting them to measured data. A paper about \acs{limpid} is currently in preparation; the following is a brief description of the algorithm behind it.

\acs{limpid} allows the determination of parameters like the positron diffusion length $L_{\mathrm{eff}}$ or the layer thicknesses of thin-layer systems. It is based on an algorithm that uses a discretized, time-independent diffusion model in which positrons jump from one interval boundary to another with a chance of annihilating in the interval inbetween. The algorithm is divided into two major steps.

A preparational step includes the creation of intervals which divide the sample into homogeneous sections in terms of, e.g., density or defect concentration. In the case of a multilayered sample we have to combine individual implantation profiles.

Now the fraction of positrons that diffuses to one of the two neighboring borders has to be calculated for every interval. This is done by solving the time-independent diffusion equation

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: diffusion equation}
    0 = D_+ \frac{d^2c(z)}{dz^2} - \mu c(z) + P(z)
\end{equation}

with the source term $P(z)$, which is obtained from the implantation profile, and the diffusion coefficient $D_+$. The respective positron concentrations at the boundaries $c_\mathrm{left, right}$ (see figure \ref{fig: limpid}) are a result of applying Fick's first law of diffusion. Finally, the annihilated fraction $a$ is found by subtracting the border fraction from the implanted positron fraction:

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: ann frac}
    a = \int_0^d P(z) dz - c_\mathrm{left} - c_\mathrm{right},
\end{equation} 

where $d$ is the interval thickness. By adding $c_\mathrm{left}$ and $c_\mathrm{right}$ from neighboring intervals we get the discrete positron concentration at each boundary.


\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.5\textwidth]{Images/limpid.png}
    \caption[\acs{limpid}: diffusion process within an interval.]{By solving the time-independent diffusion equation and applying Fick's first law, we get the concentration of positrons $c_\mathrm{left}$ and $c_\mathrm{right}$ populating the interval boundaries. The remaining fraction $a$ annihilates before reaching the interval boundaries \cite{michiBA}.}
    \label{fig: limpid}
\end{figure}

In the second part of the algorithm we iterate once over all boundaries starting at the surface. The diffusion between the interval boundaries is described by a simplified diffusion equation without a source term:

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: diffusion equation 2}
    0 = D_+ \frac{d^2c(z)}{dz^2} - \mu c(z).
\end{equation}

The diffusion rate to the next (right sided) interval boundary as well as the rate of annihilation for the right sided diffusion are again determined by Fick's first law. Due to symmetry of the diffusion process the analog quantities for the left side of the interval boundary are identical to the right-sided values of the previous interval. In conclusion, each iteration step calculates the fractions of positrons that

\begin{itemize}
\setlength\itemsep{0em}
    \item are able to back-diffuse toward the surface (to the left), %sicher?? Lucian fragen
    \item annihilate to the left of the current boundary,
    \item annihilate to the right of the current boundary,
    \item and that are able to diffuse to the subsequent boundary (to the right).
\end{itemize}


For the calculation of the S value of the current iteration step we add the fractions of positrons that annihilate to the left or right of the current boundary and the fraction of positrons that annihilated during the first part of the algorithm (see equation \ref{eq: ann frac}), each multiplied with their respective S values, to the average.

Finally, the S parameter for an implantation energy is calculated as the weighted sum of the $S^i$ values of each interval. If desired, an epithermal correction can be applied after the algorithm finished. A certain fraction of implanted, non-thermalized positrons are epithermal and contribute to a distinct $S_\mathrm{epi}$. \cite{michiBA}


\acs{limpid} deals with different positron affinities in multi-layer systems in the following way: The positron affinity of a material is attributed as an additional parameter to its layer object. It is applied in the second step of the algorithm where the probabilities for the diffusion %jump probability?
to the right and left of the current boundary are calculated. The diffusion probability is multiplied by the static Boltzmann factor $\exp{\Bigl(-\frac{A_+}{k_\mathrm{B}T}\Bigr)}$. This only has an effect on boundaries located between two layers with different positron affinities $A_+$.


\section{Ion Beam Analysis (\acs{iba})}

In the field of Ion Beam Analysis (\acs{iba}), $\si{MeV}$ ion beams are used to probe the composition and obtain depth profiles in the near-surface region of solids. Even trace amounts of particles in a material can be detected.
\acs{iba} does not require tedious or destructive sample preparation. The \acs{iba} methods used in this work are Rutherford Backscattering Spectrometry (\acs{rbs}) and Nuclear Reaction Analysis (\acs{nra}). 

\subsection{Rutherford Backscattering Spectrometry (\acs{rbs})} \label{ch: rbs}

\acs{rbs} is based on the assumption that incident ion and target nucleus only interact via the Coulomb force. It enables in particular the detection of heavy chemical elements by measuring the energy of light backscattered projectile ions. The energy measurement can be used to determine the mass of the scattering atom and, more importantly, the depth at which the atom is located. Therefore, \acs{rbs} can provide depth profiles of individual chemical elements \cite[p. 45]{wang2010handbook}.

Probe ions scatter elastically with energy characteristic of the mass of the target atoms. It has to be considered that the ions also lose energy passing into and out of the film material. In a typical $\acs{rbs}$ spectrum, the counts (number of detected backscattered ions) are plotted against the channels of the detector with the channel numbers being proportional to the backscattered ion energy. Ions scattered from heavier elements have higher energies and will appear at higher channel numbers compared to ions scattered from elements with lower mass. The high energy edge of a peak corresponds to ions scattered at the surface of the layer. If the incident ions have sufficient energy to probe the whole layer, the thickness of the layer leads to a certain peak width since the ions lose energy on their way in and out of the target.

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \begin{subfigure}[c]{0.39\textwidth}
        \centering
        \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{Images/RBS_sample.pdf}
    \end{subfigure}
    \hspace{-0.3cm}
    \begin{subfigure}[c]{0.6\textwidth}
        \centering
        \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{Images/RBS_simulation.pdf}
    \end{subfigure}
    \caption[Simulated \acs{rbs} spectrum for a $\SI{1}{MeV}$ $^4\mathrm{He}$ ion beam impinging on Cu/Cr/Si.]{SIMNRA \cite{mayer} simulation of the \acs{rbs} spectrum for a $\SI{1}{MeV}$ \isotope[4]{He} ion beam impinging on a target with a \SI{100}{nm} Cu layer on a \SI{100}{nm} Cr layer on a Si substrate. The incident angle is $\SI{0}{\degree}$ and the scattering angle is $\theta = \SI{165}{\degree}$. As Cu is the top layer and has a higher atomic mass than Cr, the Cu signal appears at higher E, i.e. at larger channel numbers.}
    \label{fig: RBSsimulation}
\end{figure}

The simulated backscattering spectrum for a bilayer on a low-mass substrate is shown in figure \ref{fig: RBSsimulation}. In this example, a $\SI{1}{MeV}$ \isotope[4]{He} ion beam is backscattered from a Cu/Cr/Si target. The high energy edge of the Cu ($m=\SI{63.5}{u}$) signal appears at higher channel numbers because it is heavier than Cr ($m=\SI{52}{u}$). Before and after interacting with Cr atoms, the incident ions lose energy passing through the top layer. Thus, the Cr signal is shifted towards lower channel numbers compared to the signal of a Cr layer at the surface. The same holds for the Si signal being broad due to its large thickness. The incident beam is stopped completely in the Si bulk.

The kinematics of the collision, i.e., conservation of momentum and kinetic energy can be described by the kinematic factor

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: kinematic factor}
    K = \biggl[\frac{(M_2^2-M_1^2\sin^2{\theta})^{1/2}+M_1\cos{\theta}}{M_1 + M_2}\biggr]^2,
\end{equation}

where $\theta$ is the scattering angle in the laboratory frame and $M_1$ and $M_2$ are the masses of the incident and target particle, respectively. $M_1$ and $\theta$ are known parameters specific to the experimental setup. $K$ can be calculated by measuring the energy $E_1$ of the scattered ion and knowing the incident ion laboratory kinetic energy $E_0$:

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: kinematic factor2}
    K = \frac{E_1}{E_0}.
\end{equation}

More accurately, $E_1$ corresponds to the high energy side of the recorded peak.

The energy of a particle which passed through a material with thickness $x$ can in general be expressed as

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: stoppingpower}
    E(x) = E_0 - \int_0^x \frac{dE}{dx'}(E(x'), x') dx',
\end{equation}

where $E_0$ is the particle's energy at the surface and $\frac{dE}{dx'}(E(x'), x')$ is the energy loss per depth, called stopping power.

The areal density, $N\cdot t$, in atoms per unit area, can be determined using the following equation:

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: areal density}
    N\cdot t = \frac{A \cos{\theta}}{Q \, \Omega \, \sigma(E,\theta)} \, ,
\end{equation}

where $\Omega$ is the solid angle of the detector, $A$ is the integrated peak counts for $Q$ incident ions and $\sigma(E,\theta)$ is the cross section. Conversion of an areal density into a physical film thickness $t$ requires knowledge of the relevant atomic densities (atoms per unit volume) \cite[pp. 45f]{wang2010handbook}:

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: film thickness}
    N= \frac{\rho N_A}{M}.
\end{equation}

$\rho$ is the mass density of the film, $N_A$ is Avogadro's number and $M$ is the molar mass. Expressing the thickness in terms of an areal density avoids the problem of determining the density of the material which may vary as a function of thickness.

The backscattering cross sections for \isotope[4]{He} ions incident on all elements more massive than Be can be well approximated by Rutherford cross sections in the energy region between $1$ and $\SI{2}{MeV}$. The depth resolution of \acs{rbs} is typically in the range of a few nanometers up to a few tens of nanometers \cite[p. 46]{wang2010handbook}.

\subsection{Nuclear Reaction Analysis (\acs{nra})}

\acs{nra} allows the isotope sensitive detection of traces of light elements in a heavier material. Like \acs{rbs}, it exploits the interactions of ions with the target nuclei for analysis. In contrast to \acs{rbs}, where the particle-nucleus collision is elastic and mediated by the Coulomb force, the interaction is dominated by the strong interaction, i.e. nuclear forces. \acs{nra} is based on inelastic scattering processes between nuclei. It is often almost background-free due to the high energy of the reaction products \cite[p. 127]{wang2010handbook}.


In this work the \isotope[12]{C}(\isotope[3]{He}, p)\isotope[14]{N} and \isotope[16]{O}(\isotope[3]{He}, p)\isotope[18]{F} reactions are used to determine the O and C contents of the metallic thin-film systems. The initial energy of the particle, in this case the proton, produced by the nuclear reaction at a certain incident ion energy and its stopping power in the material need to be known. Each peak can then be assigned to one of the nuclear reactions. Finally, the respective integrated peak counts allow us to determine the O and C contents in areal densities.

chapters/experimental.tex
\chapter{Experimental Setups}\label{chapter: experimentalsetups}

\section{Monoenergetic Laboratory Positron Beam} \label{ch: labbeam}

Within the framework of this work, we perform \acs{dbs} measurements on metallic thin-film systems at the monoenergetic laboratory positron beam of the TUM research group "Physics with Positrons", in the following called \acs{lab-beam}. A schematic drawing of the experimental setup is shown in figure \ref{fig: labbeam}. The \acs{lab-beam} consists of a \isotope[22]{Na} positron source, an ultra high vacuum (\acs{uhv}) system, several electromagnetic components for beam guidance, a sample chamber and two high purity Germanium detectors (\acs{hpge}).

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{Images/labbeam.pdf}
    \caption[Schematic drawing of the \acs{lab-beam} experimental setup.]{Schematic drawing of the experimental setup of the monoenergetic laboratory beam for Doppler-broadening spectroscopy of the positron annihilation line (\acs{lab-beam}) at TUM.}
    \label{fig: labbeam}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Positron Source and Moderator}
The \isotope[22]{Na} source of the \acs{lab-beam} was purchased in 2011. Given its half-life of $\SI{2.6}{a}$, it now exhibits an activity of $\SI{97}{MBq}$ (June 2022). It is integrated in a capsule which in turn is mounted on a transfer rod.
Since positrons originating from $\upbeta^+$ decay exhibit a broad, continuous energy distribution up to $\SI{545}{keV}$, a $\SI{1}{\micro m}$ thick W moderator foil with dimensions $10 \times \SI{15}{mm^2}$ in front of the source is used to slow them down to a low-energy monoenergetic positron-beam. The advantages of using a moderator are the positrons being emitted normal to the surface and the energy width of the beam being extremely narrow (< \SI{0.1}{eV}) \cite[pp. 712f]{schultz}. During the measurements performed, a positive bias of $U_{\mathrm{mod}} = \SI{30}{V}$ was applied to the moderator to facilitate beam guidance. To minimize adsorption of residual gas, the moderator chamber is constantly held at pressures $p<10^{-7}\, \si{mbar}$.


\subsection{Vacuum System}
The vacuum system of the \acs{lab-beam} includes a scroll pump, capable of operating a vacuum in the order of $10^{-3}\,\si{mbar}$. It is directly connected to the source coffin, which is separated from the moderator chamber by a $\SI{5}{\micro m}$ Ti foil. The scroll pump generates the pre-vacuum for two turbo molecular pumps (\acs{tmp}'s) located at the moderator chamber and the sample chamber, respectively. A manual valve for each \acs{tmp} can separate it from the pre-vacuum system. When changing samples, the goal is to vent the least volume possible. After mounting a specimen, the vacuum  in the sample chamber has usually reached a suitable level ($3$ - $\SI{4e-7}{mbar}$) after a few hours.

\subsection{Beam Guidance}
Numerous magnetic coils are installed to guide positrons from the source to the sample chamber. There are four solenoids, three Helmholtz pairs, nine flange coils and five correction coil pairs. The electrostatic guiding system consists of the moderator, four electrodes and the biasable sample holder. The positron beam energy is determined by the potential difference between moderator and sample holder as well as the moderator work function ($\Phi^+_\mathrm{W} = \SI[separate-uncertainty=true]{-3\pm1}{eV}$) \cite[p. 137]{coleman}. The sample holder potential can be varied from $0$ to $\SI{35}{kV}$ using a high voltage (\acs{hv}) power supply. Thus, the positron implantation energy covers a range from $\SI{33}{eV}$ to $\SI{35}{keV}$.

\subsection{Sample Chamber}
As can be seen from figure \ref{fig: labbeam}, the sample chamber is attached to the beamline from below. Figure \ref{fig: sample chamber} shows a more detailed cross section through the center of the sample chamber. All samples were mounted on sample mountings out of Fe which are magnetized when exposed to a magnetic field. An increased magnetic field strength at the position of our sample is desirable as it effectively focuses our beam by adiabatic beam guiding. The Fe sample mountings are inserted into an Al sample holder. The sample holder is connected to the \acs{hv} feed-through. The sample chamber is surrounded by two detector "cups" housing two \acs{hpge} detectors close to the sample spot.

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.48\textwidth]{Images/sample_chamber.pdf}
    \caption[Schematic drawing of the cross section of the \acs{lab-beam} sample chamber.]{Schematic drawing of the vertical cross section of the sample chamber. The distance between the two detector "cups" is \SI{5}{cm}; all other sizes are true to scale.}
    \label{fig: sample chamber}
\end{figure}
\subsection{Detection System}

The gamma spectrum of the positron annihilation radiation is commonly recorded with a \acs{hpge} detector with a high energy resolution of about $\SI{1.4}{keV}$ at $\SI{511}{keV}$. This is necessary since the Doppler shift of positron annihilation quanta is small ($|\Delta E|$ $< \SI{1.5}{keV}$ for $E_\mathrm{e^-}<\SI{10}{eV}$). The passage of ionizing radiation ($\gamma$-quanta in case of \acs{dbs}) creates electron-hole pairs in semiconductor detectors. Under the influence of an electric field, the electrons and holes created in the depletion zone of the Ge-crystal travel to the electrodes. The number of electron-hole pairs is proportional to the energy of the deposited $\gamma$-photons. \acs{hpge} detectors require cooling to liquid nitrogen temperatures when a high voltage is applied during measurements \cite[p. 215]{leo}. The energy resolution as well as the signal-to-noise ratio are the most important factors for high-precision gamma-spectroscopy, such as \acs{dbs}. The energy resolution is usually given in terms of the full width at half maximum (FWHM) of photopeaks, specifying the minimum separation two distinguishable peaks must have.


\section{The \acs{rks} Setup at the Tandem Accelerator at \acs{ipp}}

The \acs{iba} measurements were performed at the Tandem Accelerator Laboratory run by the Max Planck Institute for Plasma Physics (\acs{ipp}). The ion beams used for \acs{nra} and \acs{rbs} analysis are generated by a $\SI{3}{MV}$ tandem accelerator. It has a radio frequency ion source to create \isotope[3]{He} and \isotope[4]{He} ions.

\begin{figure}
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.6\textwidth]{Images/RKS.png}
    \caption{Top view of the \acs{rks} Setup with various detectors \cite{wielunska}.}
    \label{fig: RKS_photo}
\end{figure}

One of the beam lines at the tandem accelerator leads to the \acs{rks} (Rückstreutopf) setup which is a scattering chamber with various detectors for \acs{rbs}, \acs{nra} and Elastic Recoil Detection (see figure \ref{fig: RKS_photo}). It is equipped with a loadlock, which enables sample transfer to the measurement chamber without breaking the vacuum, and a manipulator for vertical sample positioning. The angle of incidence on the sample can be varied by rotating the sample around the vertical axis.

The beam current is monitored to determine the total collected charge during measurements, and hence we get the number of incident ions. The target holder is surrounded by a negatively biased (\SI{-240}{V}) Faraday shield which suppresses secondary electron emission from the target. A second, grounded Faraday shield is placed around the first in order to shield the electric field. Due to the double Faraday screen, the ion beam current measurement is accurate within a few percent. %wiki

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.7\textwidth]{Images/RKS_setup.pdf}
    \caption[Geometries of the \acs{rks} setup: \acs{rbs} and \acs{nra} configurations.]{Geometries of the \acs{rks} setup: a)  \acs{rbs} configuration in Cornell geometry where the backscattering detector ($\theta = \SI{165}{\degree}$) is directly under the incident beam. Incident beam, exit beam and sample rotation axis are in the same plane. b) Top view of the \acs{nra} configuration with detector at \SI{135}{\degree}. The double Faraday screen provides a more accurate ion beam current measurement.}
    \label{fig: RKS_setup}
\end{figure}

One of the analysis options of the \acs{rks} station is \acs{rbs}. The \acs{rbs} detector at $\SI{165}{\degree}$ is placed at a distance of $\SI{64.1}{mm}$ from the target. Taking into account the slit area we get a detector solid angle of $\SI{1.1}{msr}$. The backscattering counter at $\SI{165}{\degree}$ is in Cornell geometry which means that incident beam, exit beam and the sample rotation axis are in the same plane (see figure \ref{fig: RKS_setup}a). The exit angle $\beta$ is given by

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: exit angle}
    \cos \beta = -\cos\theta \cos\alpha.
\end{equation}


\acs{nra} measurements can be performed in the same analysis chamber as \acs{rbs}. A schematic drawing of the setup in \acs{nra} geometry is depicted figure \ref{fig: RKS_setup}b. For this purpose, we use a detector at $\SI{135}{\degree}$ intended for detecting the protons produced in a nuclear reaction. The detector is placed so that incident beam, exit beam and the surface normal are in the same plane. It is located at a distance of $\SI{37.3}{mm}$ from the target and has a parabolic slit with a slit width of $\SI{3}{mm}$ and a height of $\SI{17}{mm}$. It has a measured solid angle of $\SI{29.18}{msr}$ for maximum sensitivity. Stopper foils are installed in front of the proton detector to filter out the backscattered \isotope[3]{He} ions. The foil consists of $\SI{12}{\micro m}$ thick Mylar coated with $\SI{10}{nm}$ Au and $\SI{5}{\micro m}$ Ni \cite{wielunska}.
