chapters/analysis.tex
\chapter{Analysis of Metallic Thin-Film Systems}\label{chapter: metallicthinfilmsystems}

\section{\acs{dbs} Measurements}

For each \acs{dbs} depth profile we varied the positron implantation energy from $\SI{133}{eV}$ to a maximum of $\SI{35}{keV}$ using 60 measurement points per profile. It should be noted that the measurement points are not evenly distributed in terms of energy as the mean implantation energy is not proportional to the energy. Smaller energy steps were chosen at lower energies (see e.g. figure \ref{fig: s_fit_Si711}). The average count rate was $\SI{1065}{cps}$; on average we detected about $3.3\cdot 10^5 \, \si{counts}$ counts per spectrum corresponding to $10^5\, \si{counts}$ in the $\SI{511}{keV}$ peak area.

We recorded five $S(E)$ depth profiles in each measurement which were later averaged. This approach was chosen because it allows the monitoring of the stability of the measurement. For the calculation of the average S parameter and its uncertainty we refer to appendix \ref{ch: S_average}.

The vacuum level in the sample chamber was usually between $2$ and $4\cdot 10^{-7}\,\si{mbar}$ during measurements. Spectra with artefacts due to vacuum issues were removed from the depth profiles. This was mostly the case for energies higher than $\SI{30}{keV}$. In general, at high voltages an increase in pressure of the sample chamber could be observed in the vacuum log. The samples have sharp edges which could lead to electric discharges which in turn causes a deterioration of the vacuum.


The photopeak areas used to calculate the lineshape parameters S and W (see section \ref{ch: Sparam}) are not universally defined. In this work, we use $E_S = \SI[separate-uncertainty = true]{511\pm1.1}{keV}$, $E_{W, \mathrm{left}}=\SI[separate-uncertainty = true]{507.5\pm0.5}{keV}$ and $E_{W, \mathrm{right}}=\SI[separate-uncertainty = true]{514.5\pm0.5}{keV}$.
The total extracted peak area ranges from $481$ to $\SI{541}{keV}$.


As mentioned in chapter \ref{ch: labbeam}, two \acs{hpge} detectors are mounted to the left and right of the sample chamber, respectively. In the following only data from one of the two detectors \footnote{Ortec GEM30P} were evaluated since the energy resolution of a detector affects the S parameter. Therefore, data obtained from different detectors are not directly comparable. During the measurements performed the energy resolution of the detector was between $1.4$ and $\SI{1.6}{keV}$ at $\SI{511}{keV}$.
%This corresponds to a relative resolution of approximately $0.3\%$. 

Each depth profile was fitted using the analysis software \acs{limpid} (see section \ref{ch: limpid}). The Makhov parameters used as input parameters are listed in table \ref{tab: Makhov parameters}. For the evaluation of the data from samples containing a Cr layer, it should be noted that there are no literature values available for the Makhov parameters of Cr. Instead, they were calculated as the mean of the respective values for Ti and Fe since they are the closest neighbours in the periodic table whose Makhov parameters are known \cite{dryzek}. Hereby we obtain $A=2.74$, $n=1.6735$ and $m=1.7595$ as parameters for Cr. For the analysis of all depth profiles except the one of the Si substrate, we fixed the diffusion coefficient to $\SI{1}{\frac{cm^2}{s}}$ which is applicable for most metals at room temperature \cite{PhysRevB.41.6227}.

The lines in the $S(E)$ plots shown below represent fits to the data. The surface and bulk S parameters as obtained from the respective fits are marked in each $S(W)$ diagram.


\subsection{The Si Substrate}
For the Si substrate, the recording time per measurement point was $\SI{5}{min}$. The $S(E)$ depth profile covers implantation energies from $\SI{133}{eV}$ to $\SI{31.7}{keV}$.

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics{Images/s_w_combined_Si711.pdf}
    \caption[$S(E)$ depth profile and $S(W)$ plot of Si measured at the \acs{lab-beam}.]{A) $S(E)$ depth profile of the Si substrate. The line represents a fit to the data. B) S as a function of W. The fit parameters $S_\mathrm{surf}$ and $S_\mathrm{bulk}$ are indicated and connected by a dashed line.}
    \label{fig: s_fit_Si711}
\end{figure}

The $S(E)$ depth profile of the Si substrate including a fit to the data performed by \acs{limpid} is plotted in figure \ref{fig: s_fit_Si711}A. The depth profile displays a lower value of S and, as the implantation energy increases, shows a higher value of S at the surface. The S parameter seems to converge for energies higher than $\SI{30}{keV}$. The fit curve matches well with the measured data. Figure \ref{fig: s_fit_Si711}B shows S plotted as a function of W. The $S(W)$ points at different implantation energies approximately lie on a straight line.

In the case of the Si substrate, \acs{limpid} returned the following values for the surface and bulk S parameters as well as the effective positron diffusion length: $S_{\mathrm{surf}}=0.6337\pm0.0003$, $S_{\mathrm{bulk}}=0.6663\pm0.0002$ and $L_{\mathrm{eff}}=\SI[separate-uncertainty=true]{457\pm12}{nm}$. The latter two values are used as fixed parameters in the evaluation of all following measurements.


The $S(W)$ data points for very low and very high implantation energies coincide with the surface and bulk annihilation states as determined by \acs{limpid}. This means, in turn, that the $S(E)$ depth profile starts at surface state and eventually reaches the bulk state at high implantation energies. The fact that the $S(W)$ points lie on a straight line is an indicator of the homogeneity of the Si substrate in terms of defect concentration and the number of defects present in the sample \cite{krause-rehberg}. The diffusion length of $\SI[separate-uncertainty=true]{457\pm12}{nm}$ suggests a very low defect concentration of the substrate considering that the diffusive motion of defect-free crystals is usually in the order of $\SI{100}{nm}$ \cite{hugimage}.


\subsection{Monolayer Samples} \label{ch: monolayers}

\subsubsection{Cu/Si Sample}
The recording time per measurement point in case of the Cu/Si sample was around $\SI{5}{min}$. Due to discharges at high voltages, data points belonging to implantation energies higher than $\SI{25}{keV}$ are not included in the following diagrams and were not taken into account for the fits. 

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics{Images/s_w_combined_CuSi688.pdf}
    \caption[$S(E)$ depth profile and $S(W)$ plot of Cu/Si measured at the \acs{lab-beam}.]{A) $S(E)$ depth profile of the Cu/Si sample. Lines represent fits to the data: (a) considering positron affinities from table \ref{tab: affinities}, (b) with positron affinities and epithermal correction enabled, (c) without epithermal correction and without considering positron affinities. B) $S(W)$ plot including the points corresponding to surface and Cu bulk annihilation as well as the Si bulk value from the previous analysis. The points are connected by dashed lines.}
    \label{fig: s_fit_CuSi688}
\end{figure}

Figure \ref{fig: s_fit_CuSi688}A shows the $S(E)$ depth profile of the physical vapor-deposited Cu film on a Si substrate. The $S(E)$ profile starts with an S value of $0.630$, reaches its minimum value of $0.581$ at an implantation energy of $\SI{10.6}{keV}$ and then rises again.

We performed three different fits (see figure \ref{fig: s_fit_CuSi688}A): (a) takes into account the different positron affinities from table \ref{tab: affinities}, (b) additionally includes the epithermal correction and (c) was fitted neglecting the different positron affinities (or rater with all $A_+$ set to $\SI{0}{eV}$). All three fits match the data quite well. The only noteworthy divergence occurs at very low implantation energies where the data points can only be modeled correctly if we include the epithermal correction. Thus, fit (b) shows the least deviation from the measured data as indicated by the low value of $\chi^2$ (see table \ref{tab: CuSi688_results}).

\begin{table}[h]
%\small
\centering
\begin{tabular}{c|c|c|c|c|c|c}
Cu/Si & $S_\mathrm{epi}$ & $S_{\mathrm{surf}}$ & $S_{\mathrm{bulk}}$ & $L_{\mathrm{eff}} (\si{nm})$ & $d (\si{nm})$ & $\chi^2$ \\ \hline
(a)       & -  & $0.6264(4)$    & $0.5805(4)$        & $22.3\pm0.8$            & $456\pm3$     &  $88.4$  \\
(b)  & $0.6306(8)$        & $0.6209(7)$    & $0.5790(5)$        & $30\pm2$            & $457\pm3$     &  $42.1$   \\
(c)    & -      & $0.6262(4)$    & $0.5800(3)$        & $23.1\pm0.8$            & $343\pm2$     &  $96.2$
\end{tabular}
\caption[\acs{limpid} fit results for the Cu/Si sample.]{\acs{limpid} fit results for the Cu/Si sample: (a) with positron affinities, (b) epithermal correction enabled ($L_\mathrm{epi}$ fixed to $\SI{1}{nm}$) (c) not considering positron affinities.}
\label{tab: CuSi688_results}
\end{table}

The resulting fit parameters are listed in table \ref{tab: CuSi688_results}. All fits agree on $S_{\mathrm{bulk, Cu}}$ within their fitting errors and return similar diffusion lengths between $20$ and $\SI{30}{nm}$. The largest diffusion length of thermal positrons is obtained if we consider the epithermal correction. It should be noted that the value for the Cu layer thickness obtained from fit (c) differs significantly from the other two. We will discuss this observation below.

The rather short diffusion length of \SI[separate-uncertainty=true]{22.3\pm0.8}{nm} obtained from fit (a) indicates that the Cu layer has a high defect concentration. The value seems reasonable since similar diffusion lengths (in the order of \SI{10}{nm}) in vapor-deposited Cu films have been reported before \cite{REINER2012104}.

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{Images/imp_ann_frac_CuSi688.pdf}
    \caption[Implantation and annihilation fractions in different layers of Cu/Si.]{Fractions of positrons implanted and annihilating at the surface, in the Cu layer and in the substrate. The annihilation fractions correspond to the results of fit (a). The maximum fraction of positrons annihilating in Cu (95\%) is indicated by the vertical line at $E=\SI{10}{keV}$.}
    \label{fig: ann_frac_CuSi688}
\end{figure}

Figure \ref{fig: s_fit_CuSi688}B shows the $S(W)$ plot of Cu/Si. With increasing implantation energy, the $S(W)$ data points run counter-clockwise (as indicated by the arrows) on two approximately straight lines . Figure \ref{fig: ann_frac_CuSi688} shows the calculated fractions of positrons implanted in the Cu layer and the substrate as well as the annihilation fractions at the surface, in the Cu layer and in the Si substrate as calculated from fit (a). A maximum of $95.0\%$ of positrons annihilate in the Cu layer at an energy of $\SI{10.0}{keV}$. At the highest energy ($\SI{24.7}{keV}$) this fraction has decreased to $31.6\%$.

Taking into consideration the annihilation fractions as well as the surface and bulk fit parameters marked in the $S(W)$ plot, we can conclude that the $S(E)$ depth profile shows the transition from annihilation at the surface to almost pure Cu bulk annihilation until they finally approach a state where annihilation in the Si bulk dominates; pure Si bulk annihilation is never reached.


\subsubsection{Cr/Si Sample}

The recording time per measurement point in case of the Cr/Si sample was $\SI{4}{min}$. The minimum implantation energy was $\SI{133}{eV}$. Data points belonging to implantation energies higher than $\SI{27}{keV}$ were removed from the depth profile due to discharges appearing at high voltages.

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics{Images/s_w_combined_CrSi696.pdf}
    \caption[$S(E)$ depth profile and $S(W)$ plot of Cr/Si measured at the \acs{lab-beam}.]{A) $S(E)$ depth profile of the Cr/Si sample. Lines represent fit to the data: (a) with positron affinities and (b) not considering positron affinities whilst fixing $S_{\mathrm{surf}}$ to $0.615$. B) S as a function of W and fit results from Cr/Si as well as the Si substrate indicated in purple.}
    \label{fig: s_fit_CrSi696}
\end{figure}

The depth profile of the Cr/Si sample shown in figure \ref{fig: s_fit_CrSi696}A starts with a value of $S=0.615$, exhibits a plateau between $3$ and $\SI{7}{keV}$ with an approximately constant value of $S=0.607$ and then starts to converge to a higher $S$ value.

The different positron affinities of Cr and Si were taken into account in fit (a) but not in fit (b). The surface lineshape parameter S was fixed to $0.615$ in fit (b). Both curves agree quite well with the measured data. However, there are systematic deviations between the experimental data and the fit curve for energies around \SI{4}{keV} and around \SI{11}{keV}. As can be seen in the enlarged part of figure \ref{fig: s_fit_CrSi696}, the depth profile of Cr/Si exhibits an unusual shape showing a gradient at the beginning. This makes is difficult to fit a curve to the data. The shape of the depth profile at small implantation energies might be due to a surface oxide layer.


\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{c|c|c|c|c|c}
Cr/Si & $S_{\mathrm{surf}}$ & $S_{\mathrm{bulk}}$ & $L_{\mathrm{eff}} (\si{nm})$ & $d (\si{nm})$ & $\chi^2$ \\ \hline
(a)          & $0.6146(7)$ & $0.6073(3)$        & $6.4\pm1.9$           & $437\pm6$   & $77.6$       \\
(b)          & $0.615$         & $0.6071(3)$       & $6.4\pm1.4$          & $317\pm6$  & $63.0$         
\end{tabular}
\caption[\acs{limpid} fit results for the Cr/Si sample.]{\acs{limpid} fit results for the Cr/Si sample: (a) with positron affinities and (b) without positron affinities but fixing $S_{\mathrm{surf}}$ to $0.615$.}
\label{tab: CrSi696_results}
\end{table}

The resulting fit parameters are listed in table \ref{tab: CrSi696_results}. The bulk S values of both fits agree within their fitting errors. \acs{limpid} yields a very short diffusion length of $\SI{6.4}{nm}$ for both fits. This indicates a high defect concentration of the vapor-deposited Cr film. Similar to the Cu monolayer, the layer thickness of the fit performed without considering the different positron affinities is considerably smaller.


\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{Images/imp_ann_frac_CrSi696.pdf}
    \caption[Implantation and annihilation fractions in different layers of Cr/Si.]{Fractions of positrons implanted and annihilating in the different layers making up the Cr/Si sample. The annihilation fractions are a result of fit (a). The maximum fraction of positrons annihilating in Cr (99\%) is indicated by the vertical line at $\SI{8.3}{keV}$.}
    \label{fig: ann_frac_CrSi696}
\end{figure}

Figure \ref{fig: s_fit_CrSi696}B shows the $S(W)$ plot of the Cr/Si sample. With increasing implantation energy, the $S(W)$ data points run counter-clockwise (as indicated by the arrows) on two approximately straight lines. The top half of the figure \ref{fig: ann_frac_CrSi696} shows the fractions of positrons implanted in each layer of the Cr/Si sample. Fit (a) yields an annihilation profile as shown in the bottom half. Almost all positrons (>$98\%$) annihilate in the Cr layer for energies between $5$ and $\SI{10}{keV}$. The maximum annihilation fraction of $99.2\%$ is reached at an energy of $\SI{8.3}{keV}$ and then decreases down to a minimum of $19.0\%$ at the highest implantation energy ($\SI{26.6}{keV}$).

Similar to the other monolayer sample, we can conclude that the $S(E)$ depth profile shows the transition from the surface state to almost pure Cr bulk annihilation and then approaches the state of Si bulk annihilation. As can be seen in figure \ref{fig: s_fit_CrSi696}B, pure Si bulk annihilation is never reached.

\subsubsection*{Discussion}

We can interpret the measured depth profiles based on the \acs{limpid} fit results which are visualized in the $S(W)$ plots and the annihilation fraction diagrams. At an energy of $\SI{10.0}{keV}$ ($\SI{8.3}{keV}$) nearly all positrons annihilate in the vapor-deposited Cu (Cr) layer and hence, the corresponding bulk S parameters are directly visible in the $S(E)$ and $S(W)$ plots. On the other hand, we did not record high enough implantation energies to reach Si bulk annihilation in the $S(E)$ and $S(W)$ plots, i.e. $S_\mathrm{Si}=0.6663$ as measured for pure Si (see figure \ref{fig: s_fit_Si711}). At the highest implantation energies a fraction of 31.6\% (19.0\%) of positrons still annihilates in the Cu (Cr) layer and thus, the corresponding S parameters are a superposition of $S_\mathrm{Cu}$ or $S_\mathrm{Cr}$ and $S_\mathrm{Si}$.


For both monolayer samples, most of the $S(W)$ data points after the kink do not lie on the straight line connecting the characteristic points of the Cu (Cr) bulk with the one of the Si bulk. For increasing energies, however, the data points are approaching the connecting line again. The shape of the $S(W)$ curve in this section could be caused by an additional defect type being present near or at the interface of substrate and vapor-deposited film \cite{krause-rehberg}.

We obtained rather short positron diffusion lengths for both vapor-deposited monolayers which indicates a high defect concentration. Due to irregularities in the vapor-deposition process, i.e. variations in the deposition rate, the defect concentration in the sample is probably not homogeneous. The diffusion length in Cr (\SI{6.4}{nm}) being shorter than in Cu may be explained by the lower stability of the deposition rate during the deposition of the Cr monolayer (see table \ref{tab:deposition properties}). This leads to more defects in the vapor-deposited Cr film compared to the Cu film. Almost no diffusion occurs and most of the positrons are trapped in open volume defects shortly after being implanted. Possible defect types include grain boundaries, dislocations, vacancies and vacancy clusters.



The fits where the different positron affinities were not taken into account yield a $25\%$ ($27\%$) smaller thickness of the vapor-deposited Cu (Cr) layer. This seems reasonable considering that Si forms an attractive potential for positrons implanted in Cu or Cr leading to a small fraction of positrons annihilating in the respective vapor-deposited layer. Therefore, the result for the layer thickness is too small if the attractive potential of the substrate is not taken into account. If, on the other hand, the positron affinities are taken into account, \acs{limpid} yields a larger film thickness. The results of the two fit models would differ even more if the layers were defect-free, i.e., the diffusion length was larger.



\subsection{Bilayer Samples}

\subsubsection{Cu/Cr/Si Sample}

For the Cu/Cr/Si sample, the recording time per measurement point was $\SI{7}{min}$. The implantation energy was varied from $\SI{133}{eV}$ to $\SI{30.6}{keV}$. The measured $S(E)$ depth profile of the Cu/Cr/Si sample is shown in figure \ref{fig: s_fit_CuCrSi698}. It starts at an S value of about $0.63$, reaches a minimum value of $0.593$ at $\SI{3.5}{keV}$ and then converges to a higher S value of about $0.665$ for energies above $\SI{25}{keV}$.

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{Images/s_fit_CuCrSi698.pdf}
    \caption[$S(E)$ depth profile of the Cu/Cr/Si sample measured at the \acs{lab-beam}.]{$S(E)$ depth profile of the Cu/Cr/Si sample. Lines represent fits to the data: (a) without positron affinities, (b) with $A_+$ but without Cr layer in the sample object and (c) with $A_+$ and $L_\mathrm{eff, Cr}$ fixed to $\SI{20}{nm}$.}
    \label{fig: s_fit_CuCrSi698}
\end{figure}


The data analysis using \acs{limpid} includes three fits (see figure \ref{fig: s_fit_CuCrSi698}). Fit (a) does not take into account the different positron affinities, fit (b) does include the positron affinities but disregards the Cr layer completely and fit (c) was performed considering the positron affinities whilst the diffusion length in Cr was fixed to $\SI{20}{nm}$. This value was taken from the fit parameters obtained for the Cr/Cu/Si sample (see table \ref{tab: CrCuSi703_results}). The Cr layers of both bilayer samples were produced using similar vapor deposition parameters (see table \ref{tab:deposition properties}). Thus, they are expected to have similar properties. The resulting parameters of all three fits are summarized in table \ref{tab: CuCrSi698_results}.

\begin{table}[h]
\scriptsize
\centering
\begin{tabular}{l|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c}
 & $S_{\mathrm{surf}}$      & $S_{\mathrm{bulk, Cu}}$  & $L_{\mathrm{eff, Cu}} (\si{nm})$ & $d_\mathrm{Cu} (\si{nm})$ & $S_{\mathrm{bulk, Cr}}$  & $L_{\mathrm{eff, Cr}} (\si{nm})$ & $d_\mathrm{Cr} (\si{nm})$ & $\chi^2$ \\ \hline
(a) (*)           & $0.6262(8)$ & $0.5811(202)$ & $17.5\pm9.0$ & $52\pm50$  & $1.05\pm150.84$ & $425\pm74463$    & $162\pm32$ & $607$ \\
(b)           & $0.6304(8)$ & $0.5921(6)$ & $8.2\pm0.7$  & $139\pm1$      & - & -          & -  &  $176$   \\
(c) & $0.6289(7)$ & $0.5831(24)$ & $13.6\pm1.8$ & $71.5\pm5.1$ & $0.6376(38)$ & $20$ & $204\pm14$ & $41$ \\
\end{tabular}
\caption[\acs{limpid} fit results for the Cu/Cr/Si sample.]{\acs{limpid} fit results for the Cu/Cr/Si sample: (a) without positron affinities, (b) with positron affinities but neglecting the Cr layer, (c) with positron affinities and $L_\mathrm{eff, Cr}$ fixed. (*) Note that  fit (a) yields unphysical values.}
\label{tab: CuCrSi698_results}
\end{table}

Fit (a) shows large deviations from the measured data as indicated by the high value of $\chi^2$. It yields a larger fit error for $S_\mathrm{bulk, Cu}$ compared to fits (b) and (c). Additionally, we get a value for $S_\mathrm{Cr}$ greater than 1 which makes no sense from a physical point of view and extremely large fitting errors in general. In order to better understand this phenomenon, we neglected the Cr layer, even though it is unphysical. Only fit (c) yields a curve that matches the data in the whole energy range. Note that the fit parameter for $S_{\mathrm{bulk, Cr}}$ is 4.8\% higher than the value obtained for the Cr monolayer sample. This might be due to a higher defect concentration in this sample. Apart from this, chemical impurities could also be a reason for the deviation since Cr is not the top layer in the Cu/Cr/Si structure and therefore does not form a surface oxide layer.


\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \begin{subfigure}[c]{0.4\textwidth}
         \centering
         \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{Images/s_w_plot_CuCrSi698.pdf}
         \caption{$S(W)$ plot with fit results for the surface and bulk parameters from previous fits marked in purple.}
         \label{fig: s_w_CuCrSi698}
    \end{subfigure}
    \begin{subfigure}[c]{0.59\textwidth}
         \centering
         \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{Images/imp_ann_frac_CuCrSi698_L2fixed.pdf}
         \caption{Fractions of positrons implanted and annihilating in the different layers. The annihilation fractions correspond to the results of fit (c). The maximum fraction of positrons annihilating in Cu (80.3\%) and Cr (53.5\%) are indicated by the vertical lines.}
         \label{fig: implant_frac_CuCrSi698}
    \end{subfigure}
    \caption[$S(W)$ plot as well as implantation and annihilation fractions of the Cu/Cr/Si sample.]{Cu/Cr/Si sample.}
\end{figure}


Figure \ref{fig: implant_frac_CuCrSi698} shows the fraction of positrons that are implanted in each layer for different implantation energies. The calculation of the implantation fractions in a three-layer system is described in appendix \ref{ch: implantation}. The fractions of positrons annihilating in the different layers as obtained by the diffusion modeled in fit (c) is included at the bottom of the figure. A maximum of $80.3\%$ of positrons are annihilating in Cu at $\SI{4.5}{keV}$. The maximum annihilation fraction for Cr ($53.5\%$) is reached at $\SI{9.4}{keV}$. At this energy $65.2\%$ of positrons were originally implanted in the Cr layer. At $\SI{30.7}{keV}$, $93.9\%$ of annihilation events take place in the substrate.

For all implantation energies, the annihilation fraction in Cr is smaller than the fraction of positrons originally implanted in Cr. This is due to the different positron affinities being present in this structure. The Cr layer forms a repulsive potential for positrons since it has the lowest positron affinity and thus, the positrons preferably diffuse toward the Cu layer or the Si substrate.

The $S(W)$ plot of the Cu/Cr/Si sample and the fit results for the surface and bulk annihilation states are shown in figure \ref{fig: s_w_CuCrSi698}. The data points lie on two approximately straight lines. They run counter-clockwise with increasing implantation energy. However, the data points do not follow the path as indicated by the characteristic points for surface, Cu bulk, Cr bulk and Si bulk annihilation. A state of pure Cu bulk annihilation is not reached in the $S(W)$ diagram. This is in accordance with the fraction of positrons annihilating in the Cu layer never exceeding 80.3\%. The Cu layer is too thin or the diffusion length in Cu is too high for the positrons to annihilate solely in the Cu layer.

Similarly, the data points at higher implantation energies (after the kink) do not
approach the Cr bulk annihilation site and are far from the path indicated by the grey line. The fraction of positrons annihilating in the Cr layer never exceeds 53.5\%, and thus the state of pure Cr bulk annihilation is never reached. Moreover, the deviation of the $S(W)$ from the Cr bulk point might be due to a high defect concentration in the Cr layer or other effects occurring at the layer interface. Cr not being the top layer in this structure (as it was the case for the Cr/Si reference sample) and thus not forming a surface oxide layer could also be a reason for the deviation.


\subsubsection{Cr/Cu/Si Sample}

For the Cr/Cu/Si depth profile, the recording time per measurement point was $\SI{4}{min}$. The implantation energy was varied from $\SI{133}{eV}$ to a maximum of $\SI{35}{keV}$. The corresponding $S(E)$ depth profile is shown in figure \ref{fig: s_fit_CrCuSi703}. It starts at $S=0.617$, reaches a minimum of $0.595$ at approximately $\SI{7}{keV}$ and then converges to a higher S value of about $0.665$ for energies higher than $\SI{25}{keV}$.

\begin{figure}[h!]
    \centering
    \includegraphics{Images/s_fit_CrCuSi703.pdf}
    \caption[$S(E)$ depth profile of the Cr/Cu/Si sample measured at the \acs{lab-beam}.]{$S(E)$ depth profile of the Cr/Cu/Si sample. Lines represent fits to the data: (a) taking into account positron affinities and (b) neglecting the different positron affinities.}
    \label{fig: s_fit_CrCuSi703}
\end{figure}


We performed two different fits for the Cr/Cu/Si sample using \acs{limpid}. Fit (a) takes into account the different positron affinities of the three materials but fit (b) does not. The bulk S values of Cu and Cr as obtained from the monolayer evaluation were used as fixed parameters in both fits. Otherwise, \acs{limpid} was not able to perform a reasonable fit. The curves corresponding to fits (a) and (b) are very similar. Both agree very well with the measured data. The deviation between experimental data and fit curve at around $\SI{15}{keV}$ might be due to a different trapping process at the interface, i.e. an additional types of defects being present at the interface.
\begin{table}[h]
\small
\centering
\begin{tabular}{c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c}
 & $S_{\mathrm{surf}}$      & $S_{\mathrm{bulk, Cr}}$ & $L_{\mathrm{eff, Cr}} (\si{nm})$ & $d_{Cr} (\si{nm})$ & $S_{\mathrm{bulk, Cu}}$ & $L_{\mathrm{eff, Cu}} (\si{nm})$ & $d_{Cu} (\si{nm})$ & $\chi^2$ \\ \hline
(a)           & $0.6171(7)$ & $0.6073$        & $19.2\pm9.4$            & $68.5\pm5.4$       & $0.5805$        & $54.3\pm4.6$            & $195\pm8$ & $82$         \\
(b)           & $0.6169(6)$ & $0.6073$        & $19.4\pm9.0$            & $74.8\pm28.8$      & $0.5805$        & $1\pm27$          & $46\pm30$  & $63$   
\end{tabular}
\caption[\acs{limpid} fit results for the Cr/Cu/Si sample.]{\acs{limpid} fit results for the Cr/Cu/Si sample: (a) with positron affinities, (b) without positron affinities. $S_{\mathrm{bulk, Cr}}$ and $S_{\mathrm{bulk, Cu}}$ were used as fixed parameters.}
\label{tab: CrCuSi703_results}
\end{table}

The obtained fit parameters are listed in table \ref{tab: CrCuSi703_results}.
%Note that the thicknesses of the Cu layer differ by a factor of four.
Both fits yield the same diffusion length in Cr of about $\SI[separate-uncertainty=true]{19\pm9}{nm}$. The diffusion length in Cu obtained from fit (b) is unreasonably small and has a large fit error. The values obtained for the diffusion lengths from fit (a) are more reasonable. They are by a factor of three longer compared to the monolayer samples which is probably due to the smaller deposition rate (see table \ref{tab:deposition properties}) leading to a vapor-deposited film with less defects. 

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \begin{subfigure}[c]{0.4\textwidth}
         \centering
         \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{Images/s_w_plot_CrCuSi703.pdf}
         \caption{$S(W)$ plot with fit results for the surface and bulk parameters from previous fits marked in purple.}
         \label{fig: s_w_CrCuSi703}
    \end{subfigure}
    \begin{subfigure}[c]{0.59\textwidth}
         \centering
         \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{Images/imp_ann_frac_CrCuSi703.pdf}
         \caption{Fractions of positrons implanted and annihilating in the different layers. The annihilation fractions correspond to the results of fit (a). The maximum fraction of positrons annihilating in Cr (52.3\%) and Cu (70.7\%) are indicated by the vertical lines.}
         \label{fig: imp_ann_frac_CrCuSi703}
    \end{subfigure}
    \caption[$S(W)$ plot as well as implantation and annihilation fractions of the Cr/Cu/Si sample.]{Cr/Cu/Si sample.}
\end{figure}

The $S(W)$ curve of the Cr/Cu/Si sample (see figure \ref{fig: s_w_CrCuSi703}) passes through the point corresponding to Cr bulk annihilation and then approaches the Cu bulk annihilation site. The measurement points after the kink at ($0.030/0.593$) lie on a straight line connecting the Cu and Si bulk values.


Figure \ref{fig: imp_ann_frac_CrCuSi703} shows the fractions of positrons implanted and annihilating in the different layers. The annihilation fractions are a result of fit (a). The annihilation in the Cu layer is dominant for energies between $5$ and $\SI{11}{keV}$ reaching its maximum of $70.7\%$ at $\SI{7.8}{keV}$. The maximum of positron annihilation in Cr ($52.3\%$) is reached at $\SI{3.0}{keV}$. At $\SI{35.0}{keV}$, 96.4\% of all annihilation events take place in the substrate.

\subsubsection*{Discussion}


The fit errors given by \acs{limpid} seem unrealistically small, especially the ones of the lineshape parameters. The fit errors of $S$ are almost always smaller than $0.001$ corresponding to about 0.2\%. Therefore, the fit errors should be treated with caution.

In general, it is difficult to fit the profiles of the bilayer samples since $S_\mathrm{Cr}$ lies between $S_\mathrm{Cu}$ and $S_\mathrm{surf}$. In general, the model assumed by \acs{limpid} does not consider chemical impurities, inhomogeneous defect distributions, different types of defect or interface effects. Chemical impurities, e.g., affect the S parameter since S is sensitive to the electronic environment at the annihilation site. Therefore, including a thin surface oxide layer in the sample object would possibly lead to more accurate results. 


%S-W-plots
In both $S(W)$ diagrams, the data points almost reach the values corresponding to Si bulk annihilation. This is consistent with the fractions of positrons annihilating in Si of more than 93\% (96\%) for the highest implantation energies. The situation is different for Cu bulk annihilation. The data points only approach but never reach the Cu bulk value. There is no implantation energy at which more than 80.3\% (70.7\%) of positrons annihilate in the Cu layer of the Cu/Cr/Si sample (Cr/Cu/Si sample). Instead, the $S(W)$ data points at the turning point are a superposition of surface, Cu, Cr and Si annihilation as described in equation \ref{eq: S_layered}.


%affinities
At all implantation energies the fraction of positrons annihilating in Cr is smaller than the fraction of positrons originally implanted in the Cr layer. This can be explained by positrons diffusing preferably toward layers with higher positron affinities $|A_+|$. Cr has the smallest absolute value for $A_+$ and hence forms a repulsive potential for positrons. Due to the very small diffusion length in the monolayers in comparison to the layer thickness, no enhanced positron diffusion toward layers with higher affinity for positrons could be observed in the monolayer samples.


\newpage
\section{\acs{iba} Measurements}
For the \acs{nra} and \acs{rbs} measurements, all four samples (Cu/Si, Cr/Si, Cu/Cr/Si and Cr/Cu/Si) were mounted on a single $10 \times 2 \times \SI{0.2}{cm}$ sample holder with conductive adhesive tape. A manipulator allows the movement of the sample holder into the analysis chamber and the variation of the position along the long axis. The size of the analyzing beam is defined by two pairs of apertures in front of the targets and was set to $\SI{1}{mm} \times \SI{1}{mm}$. All calculations, e.g. stopping, backscattering kinematics and nuclear reaction kinematics, required for the simulation of the spectra were carried out in SIMNRA \cite{mayer}.

\subsection{Evaluation of the chemical purity of the samples using \acs{nra}}

First, we determine the O and C contents in each sample by \acs{nra}. The \acs{nra} measurements were performed using \isotope[3]{He} ions with an energy of $\SI{2.4}{MeV}$ as incident particles. The detection angle is $\theta = \SI{135}{\degree}$ and the incident angle $\alpha = 0$. Knowing the total collected charge ($\SI{10}{\micro C}$) and the detector solid angle ($\Omega = \SI{29.8}{msr}$), the particles * steradian can be estimated as $1.860\cdot 10^{10}\,\si{sr}$.



\subsubsection{Energy Calibration}
The energy scale of the proton detector was calibrated using the spectrum of the Cu/Cr/Si sample since it exhibits the most distinct peaks (see figure \ref{fig: NRA}c). Among these are three peaks originating from the \isotope[12]{C}(\isotope[3]{He}, $\mathrm{p_{0,1,2}}$)\isotope[14]{N} reactions.

The respective initial proton energies as well as the stoppings in the foil, which is placed in front of the detector, have to be considered. The cross sections used for the calculation of the kinematics are not available in analytical form. Instead, empirical cross sections were used. They were taken from Johnston \cite{johnston} and Guitart Corominas \cite{guitartcorominas}. The channel numbers of the three recorded peaks and the corresponding calculated energies at the detector are listed in table \ref{tab: NRAecal}.

\begin{table}[h]
\small
\centering
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & Channel & Proton energy (keV) & Stopping in foil (keV) & Energy at the detector (keV) \\ \hline
$p_0$  & 327 & 5612.2            & 313.9          &   5307.4   \\ \hline
$p_1$  & 194 & 3577.0            & 444.7          &   3132.3  \\ \hline
$p_2$  & 92  & 2158.4            & 674.7          &   1483.7   \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption[Calibration points for \acs{nra} energy calibration.]{Calibration points for \acs{nra} energy calibration. Respective initial proton energies from the \isotope[12]{C}(\isotope[3]{He}, $\mathrm{p_{0,1,2}}$)\isotope[14]{N} reactions. For the corresponding detector energies, we have to consider the stopping of the protons in the foil installed in front of the detector.}
\label{tab: NRAecal}
\end{table}

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics{Images/ecal_NRA.pdf}
    \caption{Linear fit to the \acs{nra} calibration points.}
    \label{fig: NRAecal}
\end{figure}

Plotting the calculated energies versus the corresponding channel numbers and fitting the calibration points with a linear function (see figure \ref{fig: NRAecal}) yields the following energy dependency

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: NRA calibration}
    E(X) = \SI{16.235}{\frac{keV}{channel}} \cdot X - \SI{12.640}{keV}.
\end{equation}

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{Images/NRA.pdf}
    \caption[Proton energy spectra at $^3$He incident energy of \SI{2.4}{MeV} for all samples.]{Proton energy spectra at \isotope[3]{He} incident energy of \SI{2.4}{MeV} for all samples. The fitted, blue (green) peaks correspond to protons from nuclear reactions with \isotope[12]{C} (\isotope[16]{O}).}
    \label{fig: NRA}
\end{figure}

By fitting the integrated number of experimental counts in the region of the peak resulting from the \isotope[16]{O}(\isotope[3]{He}, $\mathrm{p_0}$)\isotope[18]{F} reaction and the peak resulting from the \isotope[12]{C}(\isotope[3]{He}, $\mathrm{p_0}$)\isotope[14]{N} reaction (see figure \ref{fig: NRA}) we get the respective areal densities of O and C in each sample. The results are listed in table \ref{tab: NRAresults}. Note that the amounts of oxygen in the individual samples differ greatly from each other.

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{l|c|c}
Sample   & C content ($10^{15}\,\si{\frac{atoms}{cm^2}}$) & O content ($10^{15}\,\si{\frac{atoms}{cm^2}}$) \\ \hline
Cu/Si    & $30.5\pm4.6$ & $18.0\pm2.8$  \\
Cr/Si    &   $67.8\pm7.1$  &  $376\pm13$  \\
Cu/Cr/Si &    $43.3\pm5.5$  &  $25.8\pm5.5$ \\
Cr/Cu/Si &    $51.1\pm5.9$ &  $120\pm7$ 
\end{tabular}
\caption[C and O contents including standard deviations of vapor-deposited films as determined by \acs{nra}.]{C and O contents in each vapor-deposited films determined by fitting the \acs{nra} spectra using SIMNRA.}
\label{tab: NRAresults}
\end{table}



\subsection{\acs{rbs} Measurements with $^4$He Incident Ions}
The aim of the \acs{rbs} measurements is the determination of the thickness of the deposited Cu and Cr layers. However strictly speaking, \acs{rbs} is only sensitive to the areal densities of the nuclides present in the sample. If we assume a homogeneous atomic density (atoms per unit volume) of the sample, we can convert the measured areal density into a geometrical layer thickness.

\subsubsection{Energy Calibration}

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
Target & Channel number & Energy (keV) & Incident Beam Energy (keV) \\ \hline
Al     & 441            & 833.4      & \multirow{4}{*}{1500}      \\ \cline{1-3}
Co     & 622            & 1148.0     &                            \\ \cline{1-3}
Rh     & 705            & 1287.2     &                            \\ \cline{1-3}
Au     & 761            & 1384.8      &                            \\ \hline
Al     & 223            & 444.5      & \multirow{4}{*}{800}       \\ \cline{1-3}
Co     & 314            & 612.3      &                            \\ \cline{1-3}
Rh     & 358            & 686.5      &                            \\ \cline{1-3}
Au     & 387            & 738.6      &                            \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption[Calibration points for \acs{rbs} energy calibration.]{Calibration points for \acs{rbs} energy calibration.}
\label{tab: RBSecal}
\end{table}

First, we performed an energy calibration for the backscattering detector at $\SI{165}{\degree}$ using special calibration targets. The bulk materials installed on this target holder are Au, Rh, Co and Al. As mononuclidic elements, they have only one natural stable isotope and are therefore well-suited for calibration measurements. \acs{rbs} spectra of bulk samples begin at zero energy (or at the lowest recorded channel) and end at an element specific channel number. The channel number of the high-energy edge corresponds to the energy of the ions scattered at the sample surface. Since the target element is known, this energy can be determined using the incident ion energy and the kinematic factor from equation \ref{eq: kinematic factor}. The backscattering spectrum of each target element was recorded at two different ion energies to cover a wider range of channel numbers. The channel numbers of the high-energy edges and the corresponding calculated backscattering energies are listed in table \ref{tab: RBSecal}.


\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics{Images/ecal_RBS.pdf}
    \caption{Linear fit to the \acs{rbs} calibration points.}
    \label{fig: RBSecal}
\end{figure}

Plotting the energy versus the channel number and fitting the eight calibration points with a linear function (see figure \ref{fig: RBSecal}) yields the following energy dependency

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: RBS calibration}
    E(X) = \SI{1.740}{\frac{keV}{channel}} \cdot X + \SI{63.072}{keV}.
\end{equation}



The backscattering spectra presented in the following were measured with \isotope[4]{He} as incident ions. We recorded spectra for various incident energies $E_0$ ($1$, $1.5$ and $\SI{2}{MeV}$) and under various angles of incidence $\alpha$ ($0$, $15$, $30$, $45$ and $\SI{60}{\degree}$) for each sample. The total collected charge was $\SI{5}{\micro C}$ in each measurement. Taking into account the detector solid angle of $\SI{1.1}{msr}$, we can calculate the particles * steradian to $3.433\cdot 10^{10}\,\si{sr}$. Considering the slope of the high-energy edges enabled us to determine the detector resolution to be $\SI{20}{keV}$.



With SIMNRA it is possible to simulate the spectrum of ions backscattered from a target taking into consideration all experimental specifications described above. The simulated spectra were calculated using Rutherford cross-sections only. Dual scattering, i.e. particle trajectories with two scattering events, had to be included to correctly describe the data. The electronic stopping power data was obtained from \acs{srim}.

The O and C contents obtained from \acs{nra} were not included in the simulations since the corresponding experimental signals appear at lower energies than the Cu and Cr peaks. In addition, C and O induced stopping is negligible due to their comparatively small masses and concentrations. Thus, contamination of the samples with O and C does not affect the determination of the Cu and Cr layer thicknesses.

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{Images/RBS.pdf}
    \caption[\acs{rbs} spectra at $\SI{1.5}{keV}$ incident ion energy and $\alpha = \SI{0}{\degree}$ of all samples.]{Backscattering spectra of the mono- and bilayer samples measured with \isotope[4]{He} at an incident ion energy of $\SI{1.5}{MeV}$ under an angle of incidence of $\SI{30}{\degree}$. The areal density of the Cu and Cr layers was adjusted in a way that the simulated spectrum best fits the measured data.}
    \label{fig: RBS}
\end{figure}


Figure \ref{fig: RBS} shows exemplarily the experimental data and the simulated backscattering spectra for an $\SI{1.5}{MeV}$ \isotope[4]{He} ion beam hitting the sample surface at an angle of $\SI{30}{\degree}$. Not the whole spectra are shown since the spectra at energies below $\SI{250}{keV}$ contain not only the signal of the Si substrate but also electronic noise. We adjusted the areal density of Cu and/or Cr such that the simulated spectrum best fits the measured data. The mean of the areal densities and the corresponding layer thicknesses at different $E_0$ and $\alpha$ are listed in table \ref{tab: RBS results} for each layer.
All recorded spectra including the SIMNRA fits are shown in appendix \ref{ch: RBS_spectra}.

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{l|c|c}
& Areal density ($10^{15}\,\si{atoms/cm^2}$) & Layer thickness ($\si{nm}$)  \\ \hline
Cu/Si & $3866\pm170$ & $456\pm20$ \\ \hline
Cr/Si & $4233\pm135$ & $508\pm16$ \\ \hline
Cu in Cu/Cr/Si & $797\pm20$ & $93.9\pm2.3$ \\
Cr in Cu/Cr/Si & $678\pm18$ & $81.4\pm2.1$ \\ \hline
Cu in Cr/Cu/Si & $876\pm9$ & $103\pm1$ \\
Cr in Cr/Cu/Si & $681\pm18$ & $81.7\pm2.2$
\end{tabular}
\caption[Layer thicknesses of vapor-deposited films as determined by various $\acs{rbs}$ spectra.]{Averages of the areal density, their standard deviation as obtained from $\acs{rbs}$ and the corresponding layer thickness for each vapor-deposited layer. The conversion factors were obtained using the volume densities and molar masses. $10^{15}\,\si{atoms/cm^2}$ correspond to $\SI{0.1179}{nm}$ ($\SI{0.1200}{nm}$) in case of Cu (Cr).}
\label{tab: RBS results}
\end{table}

\section{Comparison of \acs{dbs} and \acs{rbs} Results}

We independently determined the layer thicknesses of vapor-deposited metallic films using \acs{dbs} and \acs{rbs}. The results are listed in table \ref{tab: comparison}. The two methods do not return the same values, except for the Cu monolayer where both measurements yielded a layer thickness of \SI{456}{nm}. The largest deviations occur for the middle layers of the bilayer samples (Cr in Cu/Cr/Si and for Cu in Cr/Cu/Si). However, they are still in the same order of magnitude.

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{l|c|c|c}
& Nominal thickness ($\si{nm}$) &  \acs{dbs} results ($\si{nm}$) & \acs{rbs} results ($\si{nm}$)  \\ \hline
Cu monolayer & 500 & $456\pm3$ & $456\pm20$ \\ \hline
Cr monolayer & 500 & $437\pm6$ & $508\pm16$ \\ \hline
Cu in Cu/Cr/Si & 100 & $72\pm5$ & $94\pm2$ \\
Cr in Cu/Cr/Si & 100 & $204\pm14$ & $81.4\pm2.1$ \\ \hline
Cu in Cr/Cu/Si & 100 & $195\pm8$ & $103\pm1$ \\
Cr in Cr/Cu/Si & 100 & $69\pm5$ & $82\pm2$
\end{tabular}
\caption{Nominal thicknesses and actual layer thicknesses as obtained from \acs{dbs} and \acs{rbs} measurements.}
\label{tab: comparison}
\end{table}

Differences in the results of both methods might be explained by the positron beam and the \isotope[4]{He} ion beam not hitting the sample on the same spot or due to different beam diameters \footnote{Positron beam diameter: $\approx \SI{7}{mm}$, \isotope[4]{He} ion beam diameter: $\SI{1}{mm}$}. However, additional \acs{rbs} measurements have shown that the layer thicknesses at different positions (within the size of the positron beamspread) differ by less than 3\% (see appendix \ref{ch: homogeneity}). Hence, the inhomogeneous deposition of the layers is negligible and cannot account for the deviations between the \acs{dbs} and \acs{rbs} results.

The contamination of the samples mainly affects the \acs{dbs} results and has less impact on the \acs{rbs} evaluation. In general, \acs{rbs} has very poor sensitivity to light elements in or on heavy matrices \cite[p. 46]{wang2010handbook}.
The weak elemental signatures of O and C in the \acs{rbs} spectra do not overlap with the ones of the heavier elements Cu and Cr. The calculation tool of SIMNRA \cite{mayer} based on \acs{srim} stopping power data also demonstrated that the stopping induced by O and C nuclei is negligible. Thus, the O and C contents do not affect the determination of the layer thickness using \acs{rbs}.

A significant amount of 9 at.\% O was detected in the Cr monolayer using \acs{nra}. Especially a surface oxide layer affects the \acs{dbs} analysis if it is not included in the sample object used by \acs{limpid} to model the data. In contrast, only 0.5 at.\% O was detected in the Cu monolayer. This may explain why the \acs{dbs} results for Cu/Si agree with the \acs{rbs} results but the results for Cr/Si do not.


Moreover it should be taken into account that there are no literature values of the Makhov parameters describing the implantation of positrons in Cr. Instead, they were estimated using parameters of similar elements (see section \ref{ch: monolayers}). This might lead to an inaccurate calculation of the implantation profile of Cr and in this case to an underestimation of the Cr layer thickness in the first step of the \acs{limpid} algorithm.

Furthermore, the behavior of the positron at the interface between the different layers is not known. For example, the diffusion across the interface might be largely affected by the interface itself and various defects at the interface may be formed due to a lattice mismatch \cite[p. 78]{krause-rehberg} leading to specific interface values. The $S(W)$ plots of the monolayer samples suggest that the vapor-deposited layers might be inhomogeneous in terms of defects near or at the interface to the substrate.
Nonetheless, we assumed homogeneous layers of Cu and Cr in the analysis of the \acs{dbs} depth profiles and did not account for the layer interfaces which possibly exhibit their own S parameters.

\acs{rbs} (as it was used here), on the other hand, is not sensitive to the crystal structure or lattice defects. It should be noted that \acs{rbs} gives a lower limit for the layer thicknesses since we assumed a perfect crystal structure in the conversion of the measured areal densities into actual physical film thicknesses.

In case of the Cr/Si sample we observed that both fit curves, although differing in layer thickness by 25\%, are very similar. This indicates that the shape of the depth profile is not necessarily affected much by variations in the layer thickness. In case of the Cu/Cr/Si sample the experimental data can be modeled even if we neglect the Cr layer altogether meaning that the middle Cr layer has limited influence on the shape of the depth profile. The weak signal of Cr due to its low affinity for positrons complicates the investigation of the Cr layer (thickness) using \acs{dbs} even further. In general, the shape of a \acs{dbs} depth profile depends on various parameters like diffusion lengths or bulk S parameters which makes the determination of the exact layer thicknesses of a multilayer sample difficult if not some of the parameters are known a priori. 

In general, the characterization of thin films by depth dependent \acs{dbs} is not straightforward. Their diffusive motion after positron implantation can only be described by parameters obtained from a fit to the depth profile. In contrast, the backscattered \isotope[4]{He} ions reaching the \acs{rbs} detector do not diffuse in the sample since they still have kinetic energies of several hundreds of \si{keV} when leaving the material. Their energy loss due to elastic Rutherford scattering can be described analytically. The stopping power was calculated very accurately using \acs{srim} and accounts for the energy loss caused by passing in and out of the film material.

Altogether, the determination of the layer thicknesses by fitting the \acs{dbs} depth profiles required more simplifying assumptions: We neglected the oxygen surface layer as well as inhomogeneities in the vapor-deposited layers and interface effects. Literature values for the Makhov parameters of Cr are not available and the diffusion was only modeled by a fit to the experimental data. If we assume the \acs{rbs} results to be correct, the layer thicknesses as obtained from the \acs{dbs} measurements show the largest deviations in case of the bilayer samples. This indicates that \acs{limpid} yields more ambivalent results for samples composed of more different layers.

In conclusion, \acs{limpid} yielded realistic results for the diffusion lengths considering that the samples were produced by \acs{pvd}. We were able to determine the correct order of magnitude of the layer thicknesses of all samples using fits to the \acs{dbs} depth profiles. Both methods presented are consistent in themselves, however they did not yield the same results mainly due to the above mentioned restrictions of \acs{dbs} for determining thin-film thicknesses.

    spectroscopy of the positron annihilation line}
    \acro{limpid}[LIMPID]{Layer-wise Investigation of Measurements on Positron Implantation and Diffusion}
    \acro{nepomuc}[NEPOMUC]{NEutron induced POsitron source MUniCh}
    \acro{nra}[NRA]{Nuclear Reaction Analysis}

    \acro{pvd}[PVD]{Physical Vapor Deposition}

    \acro{rks}[RKS]{RücKStreutopf}

    \acro{uhv}[UHV]{Ultra High Vacuum}
\end{acronym}

pages/abstract.tex
\chapter{\abstractname}
In this bachelor's thesis we present depth dependent measurements on vapor-deposited Cu and Cr mono- and bilayers using \ac{dbs} with a monoenergetic positron beam. \acs{limpid} is a software tool built for the analysis of depth profiles of multi-layer structures by calculating the amounts of positrons implanted in each layer and modeling the positron diffusion in the specimen. The fitting algorithm yields realistic and consistent results for the positron diffusion lengths in the vapor-deposited layers. Comparative \ac{rbs} confirms the layer thicknesses determined by \acs{limpid}. The fractions of positrons annihilating in the different layers as obtained from fits to the experimental data show that positrons in multi-layer structures preferably diffuse toward the materials with higher affinity for positrons. The results of the \acs{dbs} analysis are discussed with regard to chemical impurities and inhomogeneities in the vapor-deposited layers.

pages/appendix.tex
\chapter{Calculation of Average Depth Profiles} \label{ch: S_average}

\begin{figure}[h]
    \centering
    \includegraphics{Images/s_plot_CuSi688.pdf}
    \caption[Individual $S(E)$ depth profiles of Cu/Si measured at the \acs{lab-beam}.]{$S(E)$ depth profiles of the Cu/Si sample measured at the \acs{lab-beam}.}
    \label{fig: s_plot}
\end{figure}
%comment on reproducibility!!!

The S parameter plotted against the implantation energy in all depth profiles is the weighted average $\bar{S}$ of the S parameters from the individual depth profiles (exemplarily shown in figure \ref{fig: s_plot}).

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: weighted average}
    \bar{S} = \frac{\displaystyle\sum_{i=1} ^{n} w_i \cdot S_i}{\displaystyle\sum_{i=1} ^{n} w_i}
\end{equation}
%\cite{leo}
with the weighing factors $w_i = \frac{1}{dS ^2}$. The uncertainty $dS$ of the individual depth profiles is calculated using the Gaussian error propagation which combines the uncertainties of the two input variables $A_S$ and $A_{\mathrm{peak}}$ from equation \ref{eq: SWparameter}.
%According to the standard deviation of the Poisson distribution \cite{leo}, $u(A_S)$ and $u(A_{\mathrm{peak}})$ are given by the square root of the respective peak areas.
The uncertainty of the weighted average $\bar{S}$ is given by the inner or outer uncertainty depending on which of the two is larger. The latter becomes large when the scattering of $S_i$ is greater than its individual uncertainties suggest.

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: inner uncertainty}
    u_{\mathrm{int}}(\bar{S}) = \sqrt{\frac{1}{\displaystyle\sum_{i=1} ^{n} w_i}}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: outer uncertainty}
    u_{ext}(\bar{S}) \sqrt{\frac{\displaystyle\sum_{i=1} ^{n} (w_i \cdot (S_i - \bar{S})^2)}{(n-1) \cdot \displaystyle\sum_{i=1} ^{n} w_i}}
\end{equation}


\chapter{Calculation of Annihilation Fractions in a Multi-Layered Sample} \label{ch: implantation}

The Makhovian implantation profile must be adjusted for the implantation of positrons in multi-layer structures in a way that ensures the boundary condition of a continuous transmission at the interfaes \cite{PhysRevB.35.4606}. In general, integrating the Makhovian profile over the implantation depth yields the fraction of positrons $P_+(z)$ that is thermalized in a homogeneous layer up to the depth $z$:

\begin{equation}
\label{eq: thermalized fraction}
\begin{split}
     \int_{0}^{z} P(z') dz' &= \int_{0}^{z} \frac{mz'^{m-1}}{z_0 ^m} \exp{\Biggl[-\biggl(\frac{z'}{z_0}\biggr)^m\Biggr]} dz' \\ &= \Biggl[-\exp{\Biggl[-\biggl(\frac{z'}{z_0}\biggr)^m\Biggr]}\Biggr]_0^z \\ &= 1 - \exp{\Biggl[- \biggl(\frac{z}{z_0}\biggr)^m\Biggr]}\\ &= P_+(z).
\end{split}
\end{equation}

Using the thickness of top layer A, $z_{AB}$, we get the fraction of positrons thermalized in layer A:

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: implanted fraction A}
    P_{+,A} = P_+(z_{AB}) = 1 - \exp{\Biggl[- \biggl(\frac{z_{AB}}{z_{0,A}}\biggr)^{m_A}\Biggr]}.
\end{equation}

In case of a system consisting of only two materials, the remaining fraction of positrons, $1-P_{+,A}$, is implanted in material B.

%The requirement for combining two profiles is a continuous transition at the depth $z_{AB}$ for the integral of the final profile.
If there is a third material C, we have to combine individual implantation profiles corresponding to the different layers. First, we have to calculate the depth for profile B at which $P_{+,A}$ positrons are implanted. This is achieved by rewriting equation \ref{eq: thermalized fraction}

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: depth thermalized}
    z(P_+) = z_0 (-\ln(1-P_+))^{\frac{1}{m}}
\end{equation}

%which yields the depth $z(P_+)$ up to which the positron fraction $P_+$ is thermalized

and inserting the already implanted fraction $P_{+,A}$ in layer A with the parameters for layer B:

\begin{equation}
    \label{eq: depth thermalized B}
    z_B(P_{+,A}) = z_{0,B} (-\ln(1-P_+(z_{AB})))^{\frac{1}{m_B}}.
\end{equation}

The fraction of positrons implanted in layer B is then given by the integral

\begin{equation}
\label{eq: thermalized fraction B}
\begin{split}
     P_{+,B} & = \int_{z_B}^{z_B+d_B} P(z') dz'\\ &= \int_{z_B}^{z_B+d_B} \frac{mz'^{m-1}}{z_0 ^m} \exp{\Biggl[-\biggl(\frac{z'}{z_0}\biggr)^m\Biggr]} dz' \\ &= \Biggl[-\exp{\Biggl[-\biggl(\frac{z'}{z_0}\biggr)^m\Biggr]}\Biggr]_{z_B}^{z_B+d_B} \\ &= - \exp{\Biggl[- \biggl(\frac{z_B+d_B}{z_0}\biggr)^m\Biggr]} + \exp{\Biggl[- \biggl(\frac{z_B}{z_0}\biggr)^m\Biggr]}
\end{split}
\end{equation}

where $z_B$ is the thickness of layer B. The fraction of positrons implanted in the third layer C is again given by the remaining fraction $1-P_{+,A}-P_{+,B}$.

\chapter{Analysis of the Homogeneity of the Vapor-Deposited Layers using \acs{rbs}} \label{ch: homogeneity}
\begin{table}[h]

         & Layer thicknesses ($10^{15}$\si{atoms/cm^2}) & Maximum deviation in \% \\ \hline
     Cu monolayer & 3710, 3720, 3745, 3755, 3780 & 1.85 \\ \hline
     Cr monolayer & 4275, 4250, 4250, 4350, 4210 & 1.52 \\ \hline
     Cu in Cu/Cr/Si & 800, 800, 790, 800, 790 & 1.25 \\
     Cr in Cu/Cr/Si & 660, 660, 660, 650, 650 & 1.52 \\ \hline
     Cu in Cr/Cu/Si & 860, 860, 870, 860 & 1.15 \\
     Cr in Cr/Cu/Si & 680, 680, 655, 670 & 2.21
    \end{tabular}
    \caption[Homogeneity of the vapor-deposited layers measured by \acs{rbs}.]{Homogeneity of the vapor-deposited layers: Different layer thicknesses measured by \acs{rbs} at various sample points and maximum deviation of the obtained values. The measurement points were $\SI{2}{mm}$ apart from each other.}
    \label{tab: }
\end{table}
